INFERNO
Inferno · Canto I

Midway on the journey of our life, I found myself in a dark forest, for the straight path was lost.

Ah, how hard it is to say what kind of forest it was, this wild, rugged, and strong forest that renews fear in the very thought!

It is so bitter that it is little more than death; but to speak of the good I found there, I will speak of the other things I saw there.

I cannot well say how I entered it, so full was I of sleep at the point when I abandoned the true path.

But when I had reached the foot of a hill, where the valley that had pierced my heart with fear ended,

I looked up and saw its shoulders already clothed in the rays of the planet that guides one right along every path.

Then the fear that had endured in the lake of my heart through the night I had spent with such pity was somewhat calmed.

And like one who, with labored breath, having emerged from the sea to the shore, turns to the perilous water and gazes,

so my spirit, still fleeing, turned back to gaze at the pass that had never left a living person.

After I had rested my weary body a little, I resumed my journey across the deserted slope, so that my firm foot was always the lower one.

And behold, almost at the beginning of the steep slope, a leopard, light and swift, covered with spotted fur.

And it did not move from before my face, but rather impeded my progress so much that I was about to turn back several times.

It was early morning, and the sun was rising with those stars that were with it when divine love

first set in motion those beautiful things; so that the hour of time and the sweet season were the reason for my good hopes for that wild beast with its gay skin;

but not so much that the sight of a lion that appeared to me did not fill me with fear.

He seemed to be coming toward me with his head held high and with a furious hunger, so that it seemed the air trembled before him.

And a she-wolf, who seemed laden with all desires in her meagerness, and who had once made many people live in misery,

she brought me such heaviness with the fear that came from her sight, that I lost hope of the heights.

And as he who willingly acquires, and the time comes that makes him lose, who weeps and grieves in all his thoughts;

such did the restless beast make me, who, coming toward me, little by little drove me back to where the sun is silent.

While I was falling into a low place, before my eyes there appeared one who seemed hoarse from the long silence.

When I saw him in the great desert, "Have mercy on me," I cried to him, "whoever you are, a shadow or a real man!"

He answered me: "Not a man, I once was a man, and my parents were Lombards, both of them Mantuans by birth.

I was born under Julius, though it was late, and lived in Rome under the good Augustus in the time of false and lying gods.

I was a poet, and I sang of that just son of Anchises who came from Troy after proud Ilion was burned.

But why do you return to such boredom? Why do you not climb the delightful mountain that is the beginning and cause of all joy?

"Now are you that Virgil and that fountain that spreads such a wide river of speech?" I replied to him with a shamefaced brow.

"O honor and light of the other poets, may the long study and great love that made me seek your volume avail me.

You are my master and my author, you alone are the one from whom I took the beautiful style that has brought me honor.

See the beast for which I turned; help me from it, famous sage, for it makes my veins and pulses tremble.

"You must take another path," he replied, after seeing me weep, "if you wish to escape from this savage place;

for this beast, for which you cry out, does not allow others to pass its path, but so impedes it that it kills them;

and its nature is so wicked and cruel that it never satisfies its greedy desire, and after feeding it is hungrier than before.

There are many animals with which it marries, and there will be more still, until the greyhound comes and makes it die in pain.

He will feed not on earth nor pewter, but on wisdom, love, and virtue, and his nation will be between Feltro and Feltro.

Of that humble Italy will be salvation for which the virgin Camilla, Euryalus, Turnus, and Nisus died of wounds.

He will hunt her through every village, until he has sent her back to Hell, where envy first separated her.

Therefore, for your sake, I think and decide that you follow me, and I will be your guide, and lead you from here to an eternal place;

where you will hear the desperate cries, you will see the ancient, grieving spirits, each crying for a second death;

and you will see those who are content in the fire, because they hope to come one day to the blessed people.

To whom, if you wish to ascend, a soul will be more worthy than I: with her I will leave you on my departure;

for that Emperor who reigns above, because I was rebellious to his law, does not want anyone to come into his city through me.

He rules everywhere and rules here; there is his city and his high seat: oh happy is he whom he chooses there!

And I to him: "Poet, I beg you by that God whom you did not know, so that I may escape this evil and worse,

that you lead me there where you just said, so that I may see the gate of St. Peter and those whom you make so sad."

Then he moved on, and I followed him.

Inferno · Canto II

The day was fading, and the dark air was drawing the animals on earth from their toil; and I alone

prepared to endure the battle of the journey and of the woe that the mind that does not err will retrace.

O Muses, O lofty genius, now aid me; O mind that wrote what I saw, here your nobility will be revealed.

I began: "Poet who guides me, consider my virtue, if it is powerful, before you trust me to the high pass.

You say that Silvio's parent, still corruptible, went to the immortal world, and was there in a tangible way.

However, if the adversary of all evil was courteous to me, considering the lofty effect that would come from him, and the who and what,

does not seem unworthy to a man of understanding; for he was chosen as father of holy Rome and its empire in the empyrean heaven:

which and which, to tell the truth, were established by the holy place where the successor of the greater Peter sits.

On this journey, of which you boast, he learned things that were the cause of his victory and of the papal mantle.

Then the chosen vessel went there to bring comfort to that faith which is the beginning of the way of salvation.

But I, why come there? Or who grants it? I am not Aeneas, I am not Paul; neither I nor anyone else believes me worthy of this.

Therefore, if I abandon my coming, I fear that my coming is foolish. You are wise; understand that I do not speak.

And as is he who unwills what he willed and changes his purpose through new thoughts, so that he withdraws himself entirely from the beginning,

such became I on that dark hillside, because, thinking, I consumed the enterprise that was so hasty in beginning.

"If I have understood your word well," replied that magnanimous shade, "your soul is offended by cowardice;

which often encumbers a man so that it turns him away from an honorable enterprise, like a beast's false vision when a shadow.

From this fear, so that you may free yourself, I will tell you why I came and what I understood at the first moment when I grieved for you.

I was among those who are in suspense, and a lady called me blessed and beautiful, so that I asked her to command me.

Her eyes shone brighter than the star, and she began to say to me softly and softly, with an angelic voice, in her own language:

“O courteous soul of Mantua, whose fame still endures in the world, and will endure as long as the world is distant,

my friend, and not my friend of fortune, is so impeded on the desert slope that he has turned out of fear;

and I fear that he is already so lost that I have risen to his aid late, from what I have heard of him in heaven.

Now arise, and with your adorned word and with what is necessary for his escape, help him so that I may be consoled.

I am Beatrice, who am sending you; I come from the place where I long to return; love moved me, which makes me speak.

When I am before my lord, I will often praise you to him.” She was silent then, and then I began:

"O Lady of virtue alone, through whom the human race surpasses all contentment in that heaven whose circles are fewer,

your command pleases me so much that obeying it, if it were already done, would be too late for me; you need no longer reveal your will to me.

But tell me the reason why you do not refrain from descending here to this center of the vast place where you burn to return."

"Since you wish to explore so deeply, I will tell you briefly," he replied, "why I am not afraid to come here.

One should fear only those things that have the power to harm others; not others, for they are not fearful.

I am made by God, by his mercy, such that your misery does not touch me, nor does the flame of this fire assail me.

There is a gentle lady in heaven who pities herself for this impediment to which I send you, so that harsh judgment breaks down there above.

She asked Lucia in her request and said: "Now your faithful one needs you, and I commend him to you."

Lucia, enemy of every cruel man, moved and came to the place where I was, sitting with the ancient Rachel.

He said: "Beatrice, true praise of God, why do you not help him who loved you so much, who for you emerged from the vulgar herd?

Do you not hear the pity of his weeping, do you not see death that fights him on the river where the sea has no boast?"

Never in the world were there people so quick to seek their own good or to escape their own harm, as I, after having spoken such words,

came down here from my blessed seat, trusting in your honest speech, which honors you and those who have heard it."

After she had spoken this to me, she turned her shining eyes away, weeping, which made me come more quickly.

And I came to you as she wished: from before that wild beast I lifted you who deprived you of the short ascent of the beautiful mountain.

So: what is it? Why, why did I stay, why does such cowardice lull your heart, why do you lack courage and frankness,

since such three blessed ladies care for you in the court of heaven, and my words promise you so much good?

As little flowers, bowed and closed by the night's chill, when the sun whitens them, rise up fully open on their stems,

such was I with my weary virtue, and such good courage coursed through my heart that, like a frank person, I began:

"Oh, merciful she who helped me! and courteous you who quickly obeyed the true words she offered you!

You have so longed for my heart to come with your words that I have returned to my first purpose.

Now go, for we both have one will: you leader, you lord, and you master." So I told him; and after he had moved,

I entered the high, wild path.

Inferno · Canto III

Through me one goes into the woeful city, through me one goes into eternal pain, through me one goes among the lost people.

Justice moved my high Maker; divine power, supreme wisdom, and first love made me.

Before me were no created things except eternal, and I endure eternally. Abandon all hope, ye who enter here.

I saw these words in dark color written at the top of a door; whereupon I said: "Master, their meaning is hard to understand."

And he to me, like one of understanding: "Here all suspicion must be abandoned; all cowardice must be dead here.

We have come to the place where I told you that you would see the woeful people who have lost the good of their intellect."

And after he placed his hand on mine with a joyful expression, which comforted me, he led me into the secret things.

There sighs, weeping, and loud wails resounded through the starless air, so that at first I wept.

Diverse languages, horrible dialects, words of pain, accents of anger, voices high and hoarse, and the sound of hands with them

made a tumult, which always swirls in that timelessly tinged air, like sand when a whirlwind blows.

And I, whose head was wrapped in error, said: "Master, what is this I hear? And what people are they who seem so overcome by grief?"

And he to me: "This miserable state is held by the sad souls of those who lived without infamy or praise.

They are mingled with that wicked choir of angels who were neither rebels nor faithful to God, but lived for themselves.

The heavens cast them out so as not to be less beautiful, nor does the depths of Hell receive them, for the wicked would have some glory from them."

And I: "Master, what is so heavy on them that it makes them lament so bitterly?" He replied: "I will tell you very briefly.

These have no hope of death, and their blind life is so lowly that they are envious of every other fate.

The world does not allow their fame to be; mercy and justice disdain them: let us not speak of them, but look and pass by."

And I, looking, saw a banner that, circling, ran so swiftly that it seemed unworthy of any pause;

and behind it came such a long train of people that I would not have believed that death had undone so many.

After I recognized some there, I saw and recognized the shade of him who through cowardice had made the great refusal.

Immediately I understood and was certain that this was the sect of the wicked, displeasing to God and to His enemies.

These wretches, who had never been alive, were naked and much stung by the flies and wasps that were there.

They streaked their faces with blood, which, mixed with tears, was gathered at their feet by pesky worms.

And then I turned to look further, and saw people on the bank of a great river; whereupon I said, "Master, now grant me

to know who they are, and what custom makes them appear so ready to cross over, as I discern by the dim light."

And he to me, "Things will become known to you when we stop our steps on the sad shore of Acheron."

Then, with my eyes ashamed and downcast, fearing lest my words be burdensome to him, I refrained from speaking until I reached the river.

And behold, an old man, white with ancient hair, came toward us in a boat, crying, "Woe to you, wicked souls!

Do not hope to ever see heaven: I come to lead you to the other shore, into eternal darkness, into heat and cold.

And you who are there, living soul, depart from these who are dead." But when he saw that I did not depart,

he said: "By another way, by other ports you will reach shore, not here, to pass: a lighter vessel must carry you."

And my guide said to him: "Charon, do not fret: so it is willed there where one can do what one wills, and ask no more."

Then the woolly cheeks of the ferryman of the livid marsh, who around his eyes had wheels of flame, were quiet.

But those souls, who were weary and naked, changed color and chattered their teeth, as soon as they heard the cruel words.

They blasphemed God and their parents, the human race and the place and the time and the seed of their seed and of their birth.

Then they all retreated together, weeping bitterly, to the evil shore that awaits every man who does not fear God.

The demon Charon, beckoning to them with eyes of coal, gathers them all; he beats with his oar whoever slacks.

As in autumn the leaves rise one after another, until the branch sees all its remains on the ground,

similarly the evil seed of Adam cast themselves from that shore one by one, at signs like birds at their call.

Thus they go over the brown wave, and before they have descended from there, a new flock gathers from here too.

"My son," said the courteous master, "those who die in the wrath of God all gather here from every country;

and they are ready to cross the river, for divine justice spurs them on so that fear turns to desire.

No good soul ever passes here; and therefore, if Charon complains of you, you may well know now what his words mean."

When this was finished, the dark countryside shook so violently that my mind still bathes in sweat from the fear.

The tearful earth gave forth a wind, which flashed a vermilion light that overcame every sensation in me;

and I fell like a man who is overcome by sleep.

Inferno · Canto IV

A heavy thunder broke the deep sleep in my head, so that I awoke like one who is forcibly awakened;

and I moved my rested eyes around, raised them straight, and looked steadily to determine where I was.

True it is that I found myself on the edge of the woeful abyssal valley that gathers within itself infinite woes.

Dark and profound it was, and so nebulous that, though I fixed my gaze deeply, I could discern nothing.

"Now let us descend here into the blind world," began the poet, all pale. "I will be first, and you will be second."

And I, who had noticed the color, said: "How shall I come, if you fear, who are wont to be a comfort to my doubts?"

And he to me: "The anguish of the people below paints in my face that pity which you feel in fear.

Let us go, for the long road impels us." So he set out, and thus led me into the first circle that encircles the abyss.

There, according to what I heard, I had never wept except with sighs that made the eternal breeze tremble;

this was the grief without torment that the crowds had, which were many and great, of infants and women and men.

The good Master to me: "You do not ask what spirits these are that you see? Now I want you to know, before you go further,

that they did not sin; and if they have mercy, it is not enough, because they did not have baptism, which is the gate of the faith you believe;

and if they were before Christianity, they did not worship God properly: and I myself am one of these.

By such defects, not by any other crime, we are lost, and only so offended that we live without hope in desire."

Great sorrow gripped my heart when I heard this, because I recognized people of great worth who were suspended in that limbo.

"Tell me, my master, tell me, lord," I began, wanting to be certain of that faith that conquers all error:

"Has anyone ever emerged from it, either through his own merit or through another's, who was then blessed?" And he who heard my covert speech

replied: "I was new to this state, when I saw a powerful man come here, crowned with a sign of victory.

He drew there the shade of the first kinsman, of his son Abel and that of Noah, of Moses the lawyer and obedient;

Abraham the patriarch and David the king, Israel with his father and his children and with Rachel, for whom he did so much,

and many others, and made them blessed. And I want you to know that, before them, no human spirits were saved."

We did not stop walking because he spoke, but we still passed through the forest, the forest, I say, of dense spirits.

Our journey this side of sleep had not yet been long when I saw a fire that conquered a hemisphere of darkness.

We were still a little far from it, but not so far that I did not partly discern that a honorable people possessed that place.

"O you who honor science and art, who are these who have such honor that it separates them from the rest?"

And he to me: "The honored name that resounds above in your life wins grace in heaven, which so advances them."

Meanwhile a voice was heard by me: "Honor the most high poet; his departed shade returns."

After the voice had ceased and become quiet, I saw four great shades coming toward us: their countenances were neither sad nor happy.

The good master began to say: "Look at him with that sword in his hand, who comes before the three like a lord:

that one is Homer, the sovereign poet; the other is Horace, the satirist who comes; Ovid is the third, and the last is Lucan.

Because each one with me is suited to the name that the single voice sounded, they do me honor, and for that they do well.

Thus I saw the beautiful school assemble of that lord of the highest song who soars above the others like an eagle.

After they had spoken together a while, they turned to me with a greeting, and my master smiled at them;

and they did me even greater honor, for they made me one of their company, so that I was the sixth among so much wisdom.

Thus we went as far as the light, speaking things for which it is good to keep silent, as it was to speak there where I was.

We came to the foot of a noble castle, seven times encircled by high walls, protected around by a beautiful stream.

We passed this like hard ground; I entered through seven gates with these wise men: we reached a meadow of fresh greenery.

People were there with slow, grave eyes, their expressions of great authority; they spoke rarely, with soft voices.

Thus we withdrew to one side, into an open place, bright and high, so that we could see them all.

There, right above the green enamel, the mighty spirits were shown to me, which I exult in seeing within myself.

I saw Electra with many companions, among whom I recognized Hector and Aeneas, Caesar in armor with hawk-like eyes.

I saw Camilla and Panthasilea; on the other side I saw King Latinus who sat with his daughter Lavina.

I saw that Brutus who drove out Tarquin, Lucretia, Julia, Marcia, and Cornelia; and alone, in part, I saw Saladin.

When I raised my brows a little higher, I saw the master of those who know how to sit among a philosophical family.

Everyone admires him, everyone honors him: there I saw Socrates and Plato, who stand closest to him before the others;

Democritus, who places the world at random,
Diogenes, Anaxagoras, and Thales,
Empedocles, Heraclitus, and Zeno;

and I saw the good collector of whom, Diascorides, I mean; and I saw Orpheus, Tully, Linus, and Seneca the moralist;

Euclid the geometrician and Ptolemy,
Hipocrates, Avicenna, and Galen,
Averois, who wrote the great commentary.

I cannot describe them all in full, because the long theme so impels me that often the words fail me.

The sixth company is divided into two: the wise guide leads me by another path, out of the quiet, into the trembling breeze.

And I come to a place where nothing shines.

Inferno · Canto V

Thus I descended from the first circle into the second, which encircles less space and brings so much more pain, which pricks me to wail.

Minos stands there horribly, and snarls: he examines the sins at the entrance; he judges and sends according as he encircles.

I say that when the ill-born soul comes before him, it confesses its entirety; and that knower of sins

sees what place in hell it leads to; he girds itself with his tail as many times as he wishes it to descend.

Always before him stand many: each in turn they go to judgment, they speak and hear, and then they are cast down.

"O you who come to the dolorous hostelry," Minos said to me when he saw me, leaving the act of such a great office,

"beware how you enter and in whom you trust; let not the width of the entrance deceive you!" And my leader said to him: "Why does he cry out?

Do not impede his fateful progress: it is so willed where one can do what one wills, and ask no more."

Now the mournful notes begin to make themselves heard; now I have come to where much weeping strikes me.

I came to a place silent from all light, which roars like the sea in a storm, when buffeted by contrary winds.

The infernal storm, which never rests, drags the spirits with its ravages; whirling and buffeting, it harasses them.

When they arrive before the ruin, there are the shrieks, the lamentations, the wailing; there they blaspheme the divine power.

I understood that to such torment are damned the carnal sinners who submit reason to desire.

And as the starlings carry us on their wings in the cold weather, in a large and full flock, so that breath carries the evil spirits

this way, that way, up, down; no hope ever comforts them, not only of rest, but of less pain.

And as the cranes go singing their lays, making a long line of themselves in the air, so I saw coming, uttering wails,

shadows borne by the said turmoil; whereupon I said: "Master, who are those people the black air so chastises?"

"The first of those of whom you wish to hear news," he then told me, "was empress of many languages.

She was so overcome by the vice of lust that she made lust permitted in her law, to remove the reproach into which she had been led.

She is Semiramis, of whom we read that she succeeded Ninus and was his wife: she held the land that the Sultan rules.

The other is she who killed herself in love, and broke faith with the ashes of Sychaeus; then is lustful Cleopatra.

See Helen, for whom so much evil time was turned, and see great Achilles, who fought with love to the end.

See Paris, Tristan; and more than a thousand shades he showed me and named me with his finger, whom love had separated from our life.

After I had heard my teacher name the ancient ladies and knights, pity came over me, and I was almost lost.

I began: "Poet, I would willingly speak to those two who go together, and seem so light on the wind."

And he to me: "You will see when they are closer to us; and then pray to them by that love that leads them, and they will come."

As soon as the wind blows them toward us, I raised my voice: "O anguished souls, come and speak to us, if no one denies it!"

As doves, called by desire, with wings raised and steady, come through the air to their sweet nest, borne by their will;

such as they issued from the flock where Dido is, coming to us through the malignant air, so strong was their affectionate cry.

"O gracious and benign creature who goes visiting through the lost air us who stained the world blood-red,

if the king of the universe were a friend, we would pray to him for your peace, since you have pity on our perverse woe.

Of what you like to hear and speak, we will hear and speak to you, while the wind, as it does, is silent to us.

The land where I was born sits on the shore where the Po descends to find peace with its followers.

Love, which quickly catches the gentle heart, seized this man for the beautiful person taken from me; and the way still offends me.

Love, which pardons no loved one from loving, seized me with such a strong desire for this man that, as you see, it still does not abandon me.

Love led us to one death.
Caina awaits him who snuffed out our life."
These words were brought to us by them.

When I heard those tormented souls, I bowed my face and kept it low until the poet said to me: "What are you thinking?"

When I answered, I began: "Oh, alas, how many sweet thoughts, how much desire led these to the painful step!"

Then I turned to them and spoke, and began: "Francesca, your torments make me sad and pious to weep.

But tell me: at the time of those sweet sighs, why and how did love grant you to recognize your doubtful desires?"

And she to me: "No greater pain than to remember the happy times in misery; and this your Teacher knows.

But if you have such a longing to know the first root of our love, I will speak like one who weeps and speaks.

One day we were reading for pleasure about Lancelot, how love constrained him; we were alone and without any suspicion.

That reading drove our eyes several times, and discolored our faces; but only one moment was that which overcame us.

When we read of the longed-for smile being kissed by such a lover, this one, who will never be parted from me,

kissed my mouth, trembling all over. "Galeotto was the book and he who wrote it: that day we read no further."

While one spirit said this, the other wept, so that with pity I fainted as if I were dying.

And I fell as a dead body falls.

Inferno · Canto VI

As my mind returns, which was closed before the pity of the two brothers-in-law, which confused me entirely with sadness,

I see new torments and new tormented ones around me, no matter how I move and turn, and no matter how I look.

I am in the third circle, of the eternal rain, cursed, cold and heavy; its rule and quality are never new.

Heavy hail, stained water, and snow pour down through the dark air; the earth that receives it stinks.

Cerberus, a cruel and diverse beast, with three throats barks like a dog over the people submerged there.

His eyes are red, his beard greasy and black, his belly is large, and his hands are clawed; he scratches the spirits and tears and dismembers.

The rain makes them howl like dogs; On one side they shield the other; the wretched profane often turn away.

When Cerberus, the great worm, saw us, he opened his mouth and showed us his tusks; he had no limb to hold fast.

And my leader stretched out his spans, took the earth, and with full fists threw it into the greedy reeds.

Like that dog that barking yearns for food, and then calms down after biting, since he intends and fights only to devour it,

such became those filthy faces of the demon Cerberus, who deafens souls so that they wish they were deaf.

We passed through the shadows that the heavy rain darkens, and we placed our feet on their vanity that seems human.

They were all lying on the ground, except one who rose to sit down as soon as she saw us pass by.

"O you who are drawn through this hell," he said to me, "recognize me, if you can: you were made before I was undone."

And I to him: "Perhaps the anguish you feel draws you from my mind, so that it seems I never saw you.

But tell me who you are, who have placed yourself in such a painful place, and have suffered such pain that, if anything is greater, nothing is so displeasing."

And he to me: "Your city, which is so full of envy that the sack is already overflowing, has kept me with it in a serene life.

You citizens called me Ciacco: for the harmful sin of gluttony, as you see, I am broken by the rain.

And I, a sad soul, am not alone, for all these suffer a similar punishment for a similar sin." And he said no more.

I replied: "Ciacco, your grief weighs on me so much that it invites me to weep; but tell me, if you know, what the citizens of the divided city will come to;

if any are just; and tell me the reason why so much discord has assailed it."

And he to me: "After a long struggle, they will come to bloodshed, and the savage party will drive out the other with much insult.

Then it is necessary that this one fall under three suns, and that the other rise with the strength of one who just now bears down.

He will hold his heads high for a long time, holding the other under heavy burdens, whether he weeps or is ashamed of it.

There are two just men, and they are not understood; Pride, envy, and avarice are the three sparks that set hearts alight."

Here he ended his tearful sound. And I to him: "I still want you to teach me and grant me the gift of more speech.

Farinata and Tegghiaio, who were so worthy, Jacopo Rusticucci, Arrigo and Mosca, and the others who set their minds to good deeds,

tell me where they are and help me to recognize them; for a great desire constrains me to know whether heaven sweetens them or hell poisons them."

And he: "They are among the blackest souls; various sins weigh them down at the bottom: if you descend so far, you will be able to see them there.

But when you are in the sweet world, I beg you to bring me back to the minds of others: I will tell you no more and I will answer you no more."

Then he turned his straight eyes askew; he looked at me a little and then bowed his head: he fell with it like the other blind men.

And the Leader said to me: "He will never wake again from the sound of the angelic trumpet, when the hostile Podestà comes:

each one will see the sad tomb again, will regain his flesh and his form, will hear what echoes eternally."

So we passed through a foul mixture of shadows and rain, with slow steps, touching a little on the life to come;

whereupon I said: "Master, will these torments increase after the great sentence, or will they be less, or will they be as burning?"

And he to me: "Return to your science, which holds that the more perfect a thing is, the more it feels the good, and thus the pain.

Although this accursed people never advance to true perfection, they expect to be there more than here."

We circled that road, talking far more than I can repeat; we came to the point where it slopes down:

there we found Pluto, the great enemy.

Inferno · Canto VII

"Papa Satan, Papa Satan, aleppe!" Pluto began in a clucking voice; and that gentle sage, who knew everything,

said to comfort me: "Let not your fear harm you; for, whatever power it has, it will not prevent us from descending this rock."

Then he turned to that swollen lip and said: "Silence, cursed wolf! Consume within you with your rage.

It is not without cause to go to the depths: it is wanted on high, where Michael took vengeance on the proud husband."

As the swollen sails fall, wrapped in the wind, after the mast is broken, so fell the cruel beast to the ground.

Thus we descended into the fourth ravine, gaining further of the woeful shore that encases the whole universe's ills.

Ah, justice of God! Who can cram so many new toils and sorrows as I saw? And why is our guilt so wasted?

As the wave over there on Charybdis breaks against the one it stumbles upon, so must the people here laugh.

Here I saw people, more numerous than anywhere else, on both sides, with loud shouts, swinging weights by the force of their stern.

They struck each other; and then each turned back, circling, crying: "Why do you hold on?" and "Why are you mocking?"

So they returned through the gloomy circle on each hand to the opposite point, shouting their own shameful verse;

then each, when he had arrived, turned through his own half-circle to the other joust. And I, whose heart was almost pierced,

said: "My Master, now show me what kind of people these are, and whether these clergymen on our left were all clergymen."

And he to me: "All of them were so blind in mind in their first life that they spent no measure.

Their voice barks very clearly when they come to the two points of the circle where opposite sin separates them.

These were clergymen who have no shaggy cover on their heads, and popes and cardinals, in whom avarice uses its excess."

And I: "Master, among these such I must surely recognize some who were contaminated by these evils."

And he to me: "You entertain a vain thought: the ungrateful life that made them filthy now makes them brown to all recognition.

Forever they will clash: these will rise from the tomb with clenched fists, and these with their hair cut off.

Bad giving and bad holding have robbed the beautiful world of them, and placed them in this brawl: whatever it may be, I will not embellish it with words.

Now you can see, my son, the short joke of the goods that are entrusted to fortune, for which humankind quarrels;

for all the gold that is under the moon and that has ever been could not make a single one of these weary souls rest."

"My Master," I said, "now tell me also: this fortune, of which you touch me, what is it, that has the goods of the world so in its grasp?"

And he to me: "Oh, foolish creatures, how much ignorance is that which offends you! Now I want you to hear my opinion.

He whose wisdom transcends all created the heavens and gave them a guide so that every part shines on every part,

distributing the light equally. Likewise, for the splendor of the world, he ordained a general minister and leader

who would exchange in due time the empty goods of people after people and of one bloodline after another, beyond the defense of human wisdom;

so that one people rules and another languishes, following her judgment, which is hidden like a snake in the grass.

Your wisdom has no opposition to her: she provides for, judges, and pursues her kingdom as do the other gods.

Her permutations have no truce: necessity makes her swift; so often comes he who follows change.

This is she who is so crucified even by those who should give her praise, wrongly blaming her and calling her evil;

but she is blessed and does not hear it: with the other first creatures she joyfully turns her sphere and rejoices in bliss.

Now let us descend to greater pity; already every star that rose when I set out is falling, and too long a stay is forbidden.

We crossed the circle to the other bank above a spring that boils and pours out into a ditch that flows from it.

The water was darker than pure; and we, in company with the gray waves, entered down by a different path.

Into the marsh that bears the name of Styx flows this sad stream, when it has descended to the foot of the malignant gray shores.

And I, who was intent on gazing, saw muddy people in that swamp, all naked, with offended looks.

They were beating each other not only with their hands, but with their heads and chests and feet, tearing each other piece by piece with their teeth.

The good Master said: "Son, now see the souls of those whom anger conquered; and I also want you to believe for certain

that beneath the water there are people who sigh, and they make this water bubble at the top, as your eye tells you where it wanders.

Stuck in the mud they say: "Sad we were in the sweet air that is gladdened by the sun, carrying within us a sluggish fume:

now we are saddened by the black mist." They gurgle this hymn in their throats, for they cannot express it in complete words.

So we circled the filthy pool in a great arc between the dry bank and the middle, with our eyes turned to those who gorge themselves on the mud.

We came to the foot of a tower on the slope.

Inferno · Canto VIII

I say, continuing, that long before we reached the foot of the high tower, our eyes went up to the summit

through two flames that we saw set there, and another beckoning from afar, so far away that the eye could barely take it in.

And I turned to the sea of ​​all wisdom; I said: "What does this one say? And what does that other fire answer? And who are they who made it?"

And he to me: "Across the filthy waves you can already discern what is expected, if the smoke of the marsh does not hide it from you."

Never did a cord shoot an arrow that ran so swiftly through the air, as I saw a small vessel

coming through the water toward us in that vessel, under the guidance of a single galley-hand, who cried: "Now you have arrived, wretched soul!"

"Phlegyas, Phlegyas, you cry in vain," said my lord, "this time: you will have no more than by simply passing the tide."

As is he who hears a great deception played on him, and then regrets it, so did Phlegyas in his gathered wrath.

My guide descended into the boat, and then made me enter after him; and only when I was inside did it seem laden.

As soon as the leader and I were on board, the ancient prow went cutting through the water more than it is accustomed to with others.

While we were running through the dead stream, a man full of mud appeared before me and said, "Who are you who come before now?"

And I to him, "If I come, I will not stay; but who are you, who have become so ugly?" He replied, "You see that I am one who weeps."

And I to him, "With weeping and mourning, accursed spirit, stay; for I know you, even though you are all filthy."

Then he stretched out both hands to the boat, whereupon the wary master pushed him away, saying, "Away there with the other dogs!"

Then he wrapped his arms around my neck; He kissed my face and said: "Disdainful soul, blessed be she who bore you!

He was a proud person in the world; there is no goodness that adorns his memory: so is his shade here furious.

How many now consider themselves great kings up there who will stand here like pigs in the mire, leaving behind horrible contempt!"

And I: "Master, I would very much like to see him dive into this broth before we leave the lake."

And he to me: "Before the shore is seen by you, you will be satiated: of such desire you must enjoy."

Shortly after that I saw this man being tortured by the muddy people, for which I still praise and thank God.

All shouted: "To Filippo Argenti!"; and the bizarre Florentine spirit turned inward with its teeth.

We left him there, and I will tell no more; but a pain struck my ears, causing me to stare intently ahead.

The good master said: "Now, my son, the city called Dis approaches, with its grave citizens, its great crowd."

And I: "Master, I already select its branches there within the valley, red as if they had come from fire."

And he said to me: "The eternal fire that burns within it makes them red, as you see in this lower hell."

We finally reached the high ditches that hollow that desolate land: the walls seemed to me to be made of iron.

Not without first making a great detour, we reached a place where the ferryman loudly cried, "Get out of here!" "Here is the entrance."

I saw more than a thousand people rained down from heaven at the gates, angrily saying, "Who is this who, without death,

goes through the kingdom of the dead people?" And my wise master signaled that he wished to speak to them secretly.

Then they somewhat restrained their great disdain and said, "You come alone, and let him go who so boldly entered this kingdom.

Let him return alone by the mad road: try it, if he knows; for you will remain here, who has escorted him through such a dark region."

Think, reader, if I was discouraged by the sound of those cursed words, for I never believed I would return there.

"O my dear Leader, who more than seven times have restored me safety and delivered me from the great peril that confronted me,

do not leave me," I said, "so ruined; and if further passage is denied us, let us quickly retrace our steps together."

And that lord who had led me there said to me: "Do not fear; for our path cannot be taken from us by anyone: it is given by such.

But wait for me here, and comfort my weary spirit and nourish it with good hope, for I will not abandon you in the lower world."

So he goes, and there my sweet father abandons me, and I remain in doubt, for yes or no contends in my head.

I could not hear what he offered them; but he had not remained there with them long, before each of them ran back inside in a spirit of trial.

Those adversaries of ours closed the doors in the breast of my lord, who remained outside and turned to me with rare steps.

His eyes were on the ground, his brows stripped of all boldness, and he said with sighs: "Who denied me the woeful houses?"

And to me he said: "You, do not be dismayed because I am angry, for I will win the test, whoever turns within for defense.

This arrogance of theirs is not new; for they have already used it on a less secret door, which still stands without a lock.

Above it you saw the dead writing: and already on this side of it the steep slope descends, passing through the circles without an escort,

so that the city will be opened for him."

Inferno · Canto IX

The color that cowardice had driven from me, seeing my leader turn away, sooner withdrew its new one.

He stopped attentively, like a man who listens; for the eye could not lead him far through the black air and the thick fog.

"We must win the battle," he began, "unless . . . Such a one offered himself to us. Oh, how long it takes for me for another to arrive here!"

I saw clearly how he covered his beginning with the other that came after, which were words different from the first;

but nevertheless his words gave me fear, because I was drawing the truncated word to perhaps a worse meaning than it had.

"Does anyone from the first rank ever descend into this depth of the sad hollow, which has hope cut off for punishment alone?"

This question I asked; and he replied, "It rarely happens," "that one of us makes the journey I travel.

True it is that once before I was down here, conspired by that cruel Erithon who called the shades back to their bodies.

My flesh was scarcely naked when she made me enter that wall, to draw out a spirit from the circle of Judas.

That is the lowest place and the darkest, and the furthest from the heaven that revolves all things: I know the path well; therefore, take heart.

This swamp that exudes a great stench encircles the woeful city, where we can no longer enter without anger."

And he said more, but I don't remember it; Because my eye had drawn me entirely toward the high tower with its burning summit,

where in a moment three infernal Furies, stained with blood, stood up, with feminine limbs and pose,

and were girdled with greenest hydras; serpents and horned snakes were for hair, which bound their fierce temples.

And he, who well knew the wretched women of the queen of eternal weeping, said to me, "Look," the fierce Erines.

This is Megaera on the left side; she who weeps on the right is Alecto; Tisiphon is in the middle." And he remained silent.

Each one tore her breast with her nails; they beat themselves with their palms and cried out so loudly that I clung to the poet out of suspicion.

"Let Medusa come! We will make him of enamel," they all said, looking down; "we have not avenged Theseus' assault."

"Turn back and keep your face closed; for if the Gorgon appears and you see him, there would be no need to ever return."

So said the master; and he himself turned me, and did not hold my hands, but still closed them with his own.

O you who have sound intellects, consider the doctrine that lies hidden beneath the veil of strange verses.

And already a roar of a sound was coming over the turbid waves, full of terror, making both banks tremble,

like a wind, impetuous because of the opposing heat, that rages through the forest and, without any restraint,

smashes, knocks down, and carries away the branches; it goes proudly ahead, dusty, and scares away the wild beasts and the shepherds.

He opened my eyes and said: "Now direct the nerve of your face through that ancient foam, where that smoke is most bitter."

As frogs all flee through the water before the hostile snake, until each one sticks to the ground,

I saw more than a thousand destroyed souls flee in the same way before one who crossed the Styx at a walk with parched soles.

From his face he removed that heavy air, often waving his left hand forward; and only from that anguish did he seem weary.

I clearly realized that he was sent by heaven, and I turned to the master; and he signaled me to remain quiet and bow before him.

Ah, how full of disdain he seemed to me! He came to the door and opened it with a small rod, so that there was no hesitation.

"O exiles from heaven, you despised people," he began on the horrible threshold, "whence this arrogance takes root in you?

Why do you resist that desire whose end can never be curbed, and which has often increased your pain?

What good is it to butt heads with the fairies? Your Cerberus, if you remember rightly, still has his chin and his throat peeled off."

Then he turned back along the filthy street and said no word to us, but he looked like a man whose concern is gripping and gnawing

than that of him who stands before him; and we moved our feet toward the earth, confident behind the holy words.

We entered there without any war; And I, who longed to see the condition that such a fortress holds,

as soon as I was inside, cast my gaze around: and I see on every hand a vast plain, full of grief and cruel torment.

Just as at Arli, where the Rhone stagnates, as at Pola, near the Carnaro that encloses Italy and bathes its borders,

the tombs make the whole place hollow, so they did here on every side, except that the method was more bitter;

for among the tombs flames were scattered, by which they were so completely enflamed, that iron no longer requires any skill.

All their lids were suspended, and from them came such harsh laments that they seemed indeed those of the wretched and the offended.

And I: "Master, who are those people who, buried within those tombs, make themselves heard with mournful sighs?"

And he to me: "Here are the heretics with their followers, of every sect, and the tombs are more laden than you believe.

Here like with like is buried, and the monuments are more and less warm." And after he had turned to the right,

we passed between the martyrdoms and the high ramparts.

Inferno · Canto X

Now he goes along a secret path, between the wall of the earth and the torments, my master, and I behind him.

"O supreme virtue, who through the impious circles turns me," I began, "as pleases you, speak to me, and satisfy my desires.

Could the people who lie in the tombs be seen? Already all the lids are lifted, and no one guards."

And he to me: "All will be locked up when they return here from Josaphat with the bodies they left up there.

His cemetery on this side has all his followers, along with Epicurus, who leave the soul dead with the body.

Therefore the question you ask me here will soon be satisfied, and the desire you keep silent about it."

And I: "Good leader, I keep my heart hidden from you except to say the least, and you have not yet disposed me to do so."

"O Tuscan, who through the city of living fire go speaking thus modestly, be pleased to remain in this place.

Your speech reveals you to be a native of that noble fatherland, to which perhaps I was too unwelcome."

Suddenly this sound came from one of the tombs; so I, fearful, drew a little closer to my leader.

And he said to me: "Turn around! What are you doing? Look, Farinata, he's standing there; from the waist up you'll see him all over."

I already had my face fixed on his; and he was erect with his chest and forehead as if he held Hell in great contempt.

And the brave and ready hands of the leader drew me to him among the tombs, saying: "Let your words be told."

When I reached the foot of his tomb, he looked at me a little, and then, almost disdainfully, he asked me: "Who were your ancestors?"

I, who was eager to obey, did not hide it from him, but revealed it completely; whereupon he raised his brows a little;

then he said: "They were so fiercely opposed to me and to my ancestors and to my party, that twice I scattered them."

"If they were driven out, they returned from every side," I replied, "both once and again; but yours did not learn that art well."

Then a shadow rose up from my uncovered vision, along this one, up to my chin: I believe it had risen to its knees.

He looked around me, as if he had a desire to see if anyone else was with me; and when all suspicion was extinguished,

he said, weeping: "If through this blind prison you go by lofty genius, where is my son? And why is he not with you?"

And I to him: "I do not come of myself: he who waits there, perhaps leads me here, whom your Guido disdained."

His words and the manner of his punishment had already read his name to me; therefore his answer was so full.

Suddenly, rising, he cried: "What? You said 'he had'? Is he not still alive? Does not the sweet light shine upon his eyes?"

When he realized I was making some delay before answering, he fell back supine and no longer appeared.

But the other magnanimous one, to whom I had remained, did not change his aspect, nor move his neck, nor bend his side;

and continuing his first words, he said, "If they have that art poorly learned, it torments me more than this bed.

But the face of the lady who rules here will not be rekindled fifty times before you will know how much that art weighs.

And if you ever rule in the sweet world, tell me: why is that people so wicked towards mine in every one of its laws?"

Whereupon I said to him: "The slaughter and the great carnage that colored the Arbia red, causes such a prayer to be made in our temple."

After he had shaken his head with a sigh, "I was not alone in this," he said, "nor would I have moved with the others without cause.

But I alone, there where everyone suffered the taking away of Florence, was the one who defended her with open face."

"Ah, if your seed ever rests," I begged him, "untie for me the knot that has entangled my thought here.

It seems that you see, if I hear rightly, ahead what time brings with it, and at present you hold another course."

"We see, like those who have a bad light, things that are far from us," he said, "so much so that the supreme leader still shines upon us.

When they approach or are, our intellect is all vain; and if others do not bring us information, we know nothing of your human state.

You may therefore understand that our knowledge will be entirely dead from the moment the door of the future is closed."

Then, as if remorseful for my fault, I said: "Now then, you will tell that fallen man that his son is still joined to the living;

and if I was silent before at the answer, let him know that I did so because I was already thinking of the error you have solved for me."

And now my Master was calling me back; whereupon I prayed the spirit more eagerly to tell me who was with him.

He said to me: "Here I lie with more than a thousand: within here is the second Frederick and the Cardinal; and of the others I am silent."

Then he hid himself; and I turned my steps toward the ancient poet, thinking back on that speech that seemed hostile to me.

He moved; and then, as we walked, he said to me: "Why are you so lost?" And I satisfied his question.

"Let your mind preserve what you have heard against yourself," that sage commanded me; "and now wait here," and he pointed his finger:

"When you are before the sweet ray of her whose beautiful eye sees all, from her you will know the path of your life."

Then he moved his foot to the left: we left the wall and went toward the middle along a path that leads to a valley,

which even up there made its stench unpleasant.

Inferno · Canto XI

At the edge of a high bank formed by large broken stones in a circle, we came upon a more cruel crowd;

and there, because of the horrible excess of stench that the deep abyss spews, we approached, behind, the lid

of a great tomb, where I saw an inscription that read: "I guard Pope Anastasius, who drew Photinus from the straight path."

"Our descent must be slow, so that at first our senses may accustom themselves a little to the sad breath; and then we will pay no attention to it."

Thus said the Master; and I: "Some compensation," I said to him, "find that the time is not wasted." And he: "See, I am thinking of that."

"My son, within these rocks," he then began to say, "are three little circles, one tier after another, like those you leave behind.

They are all full of accursed spirits; but so that the sight alone may be enough for you, understand how and why they are constrained.

The end of every malice that earns hatred in heaven is injury, and every such end saddens others either by force or by fraud.

But because fraud is man's own evil, it displeases God more; and therefore the deceitful are below, and greater pain assails them.

The first circle is entirely of violent men; but because force is applied to three persons, it is divided and constructed into three circles.

Force can be applied to God, to oneself, and to one's neighbor—I mean in them and in their affairs, as you will hear with clear reason.

Death by force and painful wounds are inflicted on one's neighbor, and ruin, fire, and harmful thefts on his possessions;

wherefore murderers and all who do evil, wreckers, and plunderers, torment all the first circle in various ranks.

A man may have a violent hand upon himself and his possessions; and therefore in the second circle he must repent without cause.

Whoever deprives himself of your world, gambles and squanders his wealth, and weeps where he should be joyful.

One can exert force upon the deity by denying and blaspheming it with one's heart, and by scorning nature and its goodness;

and therefore the lesser circle seals with its mark both Sodom and Chaos, and he who, scorning God with his heart, speaks.

Fraud, which stings every conscience, can be practiced by a man against him who trusts him and against him who does not place faith in him.

This method, however, seems to cut through the bond of love that nature creates; hence, in the second circle, there nests

hypocrisy, flattery, and witchcraft, falsehood, theft, and simony, pimps, barters, and similar filth.

By the other method, that love that nature creates is forgotten, and that which is added later, from which special faith is created;

hence, in the lesser circle, where is the point of the universe on which Dis sits, every betrayer is consumed forever."

And I: "Master, your reasoning proceeds very clearly, and very well distinguishes this abyss and the people who possess it.

But tell me: those of the rich marsh, driven by the wind, beaten by the rain, and met with such harsh tongues,

why are they not punished within the red city, if God is angry with them? And if He is not, why are they in this guise?

And he to me, "Why," he said, "does your mind wander so far from what it used to? Or toward where else does your mind look?

Do you not remember those words with which your Ethics discusses the three dispositions that heaven does not want:

incontinence, malice, and mad bestiality? And how does incontinence offend God less and attract less blame?

If you consider this sentence well, and bring to mind who are those who endure penance above,

you will clearly see why these fellers have departed, and why divine vengeance hammers them less wrathfully.

"O sun who heals every troubled sight, you so content me when you resolve, that, no less than knowing, doubt pleases me.

"Return again a little," I said, "to where you say that usury offends divine goodness, and resolve the knot."

"Philosophy," he said to me, "to whoever understands it, note, not just in one part, how nature takes its course

from the divine intellect and its art; And if you study your Physics well, you will find, not after many pages,

that your art follows it as best it can, as the master does the student; so that your art is almost the grandson of God.

From these two, if you recall Genesis from the beginning, one must take one's life and advance the people;

and because the usurer takes another path, nature disdains it for itself and for its follower, since it places its hope elsewhere.

But follow me now, for it pleases me to travel; for the Fishes dart across the horizon, and the Chariot lies entirely above the Chorus,

and the cliff descends far beyond."

Inferno · Canto XII

The place where we came to descend the bank was mountainous and, for what was there, such that every sight would be shy of it.

Like that ruin which the Adige struck on the side this side of Trento, either by earthquake or by failing support,

that from the summit of the mountain, whence it started, the rock has fallen to the plain so steeply that it would give no way to anyone who was up there:

such was the descent of that ravine; and on the tip of the broken ravine the infamy of Crete was spread

that was conceived in the false cow; and when he saw us, he bit himself, like one whose anger weakens within.

My sage cried out to him: "Perhaps you believe that here is the Duke of Athens, who up in the world brought death upon you?

Begone, beast, for this one is not being taught by your sister, but is going to see your torments."

Like the bull that breaks loose in the place that has already received the mortal blow, that cannot move, but leaps here and there,

I saw the Minotaur do so; and the wary one cried out: "Run to the pass; while he rages, it is best that you lower yourself."

So we set off down the slope of those stones, which often moved under my feet because of the new load.

I was already thinking; and he said: "Perhaps you are thinking of this ruin, which is guarded by that bestial wrath that I just quenched.

Now I want you to know that the other time I descended down here into the lower hell, this rock had not yet fallen.

But surely, if I discern correctly, shortly before he came who took the great spoils from the upper circle of Dis,

the high valley trembled so fiercely on all sides that I thought the universe felt love, through which, whoever believes,

the world has often turned into chaos; and at that moment this ancient rock, here and elsewhere, made such a reversal.

But fix your eyes on the valley, for the river of blood approaches, in which boils whoever harms another through violence.

Oh blind greed and mad anger, that spur us on so in short life, and then so poorly dampens us in eternal life!

I saw a wide ditch curved in an arc, like that which encompasses the entire plain, as my escort had said;

And between the foot of the bank and it, centaurs ran in pursuit, armed with arrows, as they were wont to hunt in the world.

Seeing us descend, each stopped, and three of the troop departed with bows and spears first chosen;

and one cried from afar: "To what torment come you who descend the hillside? Tell us here; if not, I will draw my bow."

My master said: "We will answer Chiron nearby; your desire was always so hasty."

Then he tempted me and said: "That is Nessus, who died for the beautiful Deianira, and took vengeance himself.

And the one in the middle, who is looking at his chest, is great Chiron, who raised Achilles; the other is Pholus, who was so full of wrath.

Around the ditch they go, thousands upon thousands, shooting down every soul that snatches itself from the blood, more than its guilt has wrought upon it.

We approached those slender beasts: Chiron took an arrow, and with the notch he made his beard behind his jaws.

When he had uncovered his great mouth, he said to his companions: "Are you aware that the one behind moves what it touches?

The feet of the dead are not wont to do so." And my good Leader, who was already at his chest, where the two natures are consorted,

replied: "He is indeed alive, and so alone must I show him the dark valley; necessity drives us, not pleasure.

One of them turned away from singing Alleluia, who entrusted me with this new task: he is not a thief, nor am I a fugitive soul.

But by that power by which I move my steps along such a rugged road, give us one of yours, to whom we are in danger,

and let him show us where the ford is, and carry this one on his back, for he is no spirit that wanders through the air."

Chiron turned on his right breast and said to Nessus: "Return, and lead them, and make them go away if another troop encounters you."

Now we moved with our faithful escort along the edge of the red boiling water, where the boiled ones were shrieking loudly.

I saw people below, up to the brow; and the great centaur said: "These are tyrants who deal in blood and in possession.

There the merciless damages are lamented; there is Alexander, and fierce Dionysius, who caused Sicily to have painful years.

And that forehead with such black hair,
is Azzolino; and the other, who is blond,
is Opizzo da Esti, who, in truth,

was slain by his stepson up in the world." Then I turned to the poet, and he said: "Let this one be first for you, and I second."

A little further on, the centaur stood over a crowd who seemed to emerge from that boiling pool up to their throats.

He showed us a shadow alone on one side, saying: "He cleft in the lap of God the heart that still flows on the Thames."

Then I saw people who held their heads and their entire chests outside the stream; and I recognized many of them.

So the blood gradually deepened, so that it even clogged our feet; and our passage through the ditch was thus.

"Just as on this side you see the ever-deeper tide," said the centaur, "I want you to believe

that on this other side its bottom presses deeper and deeper, until it reaches where tyranny must groan.

Divine justice here stings that Attila who was a scourge on earth, and Pyrrhus and Sextus; and forever milks

the tears, which with the boiling it releases, from Rinier da Corneto, from Rinier Pazzo, who waged such war on the streets."

Then he turned and passed the puddle again.

Inferno · Canto XIII

Nessus had not yet reached the other side when we entered a wood marked by no path.

Not green foliage, but dark-colored; not straight branches, but gnarled and twisted; not apples, but poisonous twigs.

The wild beasts that hate the cultivated lands between Cecina and Corneto have neither such rough nor so thick brushwood.

There the foul Harpies build their nests, which drive the Trojans from the Strophades with grim omens of future doom.

They have broad wings, and human necks and faces, clawed feet, and great feathered bellies; they lament on the strange trees.

And the good Master began to say to me, "Before you enter further, know that you are in the second circle, and will be there until you come to the horrible sand. So look carefully; you will see things that will give credence to my speech."

I heard wailing from all sides, and saw no one making it; so I stopped, completely bewildered.

I believe he thought I believed that so many voices were coming from those holes, from people hiding behind us.

Then the Master said, "If you break off a few branches from one of these trees, all the thoughts you have will be utterly crippled."

Then I reached out a little and plucked a twig from a great bramble; and its trunk cried out, "Why do you crush me?"

After he had turned black with blood, he began to say again: "Why do you tear me apart? Have you no spirit of pity?

We were men, and now we have become thorns: your hand should be more merciful, if we were the souls of serpents."

As from a green stick that is burned at one end, and at the other groans and creaks in the escaping wind,

from the broken splinter came forth both words and blood; whereupon I let the end fall and stood like a man in fear.

"If he had been able to believe before," my sage replied, "wounded soul, what he has seen only through my rhyme,

he would not have stretched out his hand towards you; but the incredible thing made me persuade him to do something that weighs on me.

But tell him who you were, so that instead of any amends, your fame may be refreshed in the world above, where it is permitted to return."

And the trunk: "You so entice me with sweet speech that I cannot remain silent; and do not be troubled because I am tempted to speak a little.

I am he who held both keys to Federigo's heart, and who turned them, locking and unlocking, so sweetly,

that I took almost every man from his secret; I bore such faith in the glorious office that I lost sleep and my strength.

The harlot who never turned her childish eyes from Caesar's hostel, common death and the vice of courts,

inflamed all souls against me; and the inflamed ones so inflamed Augustus, that happy honors turned to sad mourning.

My soul, out of a disdainful pleasure, believing by dying to escape disdain, made me unjust against myself, just.

By the new roots of this tree I swear to you that I never broke faith with my lord, who was so worthy of honor.

And if any of you return to the world, let him comfort my memory, which still lies dormant from the blow that envy dealt it."

He waited a little, and then, "Since he is silent," the poet said to me, "do not waste the time; but speak, and ask him, if it pleases you more."

So I said to him, "Ask him again what you think will satisfy me; for I cannot, so great pity grieves me."

So he began again: "If the man freely does for you what your speech begs, imprisoned spirit, may it please you also

to tell us how the soul is bound in these knots; and tell us, if you can, if any ever frees itself from such limbs."

Then the trunk blew strongly, and then the wind changed into this voice: "Briefly will be answered to you.

When the fierce soul separates from the body from which it tore itself, Minos sends it to the seventh pass.

It falls into the forest, and is not a chosen part; but where Fortune shoots it, there it sprouts like a grain of spelt.

It grows into a swamp vine and a wild plant: the Harpies, feeding on its leaves, cause pain, and open the window to pain.

Like the others, we will come for our spoils, but not to clothe any of them, for it is not right to have what one takes.

Here we will drag them, and through the sad forest our bodies will be hanging, each from the thorn of his own troublesome shade.

We were still waiting for the tree trunk, believing that he wanted to say more to us, when we were surprised by a noise,

like one who hears the pig and the hunt coming to its post, who hears the beasts and the branches rustling.

And behold, two from the left side, naked and clawed, fleeing so fast that they broke every branch in the forest.

The one in front: "Now hurry, hurry, death!" And the other, who seemed to be slowing too much, shouted: "Lano, your legs were not so alert

to the jousts at Toppo!" And then, perhaps because his breath was failing him, he formed a knot of himself and a bush.

Behind them was the forest filled with black hounds, eager and running like greyhounds released from chains.

They set their teeth to the one who crouched, and tore him piece by piece; then they carried away those aching limbs.

Then my escort took me by the hand and led me to the bush that was weeping in vain for its bleeding wounds.

"O Jacopo," he said, "of Saint Andrew, what did it avail you to make a shield of me? What guilt am I of your wicked life?"

When the master had stopped above him, he said: "Who were you, that through so many thorns you blew a woeful speech with blood?"

And he to us: "O souls who have come to see the shameful torment that has separated my branches from me,

gather them at the foot of the sad basket. I was from the city that changed its first master to the Baptist; for which he

will always make it sad with his art; and if it were not that at the Arno Pass some glimpse of him still remains,

those citizens who later rebuilt it on the ashes that remained of Attila would have had their work in vain.

I made a gibe for myself of my houses."

Inferno · Canto XIV

After the love of my native place constrained me, I gathered the scattered leaves and returned them to him, who was already hoarse.

Then we came to the end where the second circle separates from the third, and where one sees the horrible art of justice.

To clearly explain the new things, I say that we arrived at a plain that removes every plant from its bed.

The woeful forest is a garland around it, like the sad ditch around it; there we stopped our steps, one after the other.

The space was arid and thick sand, made of no other shape than that which was once slain by the feet of Cato.

O vengeance of God, how you must be feared by everyone who reads what was manifest to my eyes!

I saw many flocks of naked souls, all weeping miserably, and it seemed a different law had been imposed on them.

Some people lay supine on the ground, some sat huddled together, and others walked continuously.

Those who were walking around were more numerous, and those who lay less in torment, but had their tongues more free to mourn.

Over all the sand, in a slow fall, large flakes of fire rained down, like snow on a windless mountain.

Just as Alexander in those hot parts of India saw flames fall solidly to the ground upon his band,

wherefore he took care to trample the ground with his troops, so that the vapor was best extinguished while it was alone:

such was the eternal ardor descending; where the sand ignited, like tinder under a steel, to double the pain.

The wretched hands never rested, now here, now there shaking off the fresh burning heat.

I began: "Master, you who conquer all things, except the harsh demons who come out against us at the entrance to the gate,

who is that great one who does not seem to care about the fire and lies there, spiteful and twisted, so that the rain does not seem to mar him?"

And that same one, who had noticed that I was asking my leader about him, cried out: "As I was alive, so am I dead.

If Jove tires his smith, from whom in anger he took the sharp thunderbolt with which I was struck on the last day;

or if he tired the others one by one on Mount Etna at the black forge, calling out, "Good Vulcan, help, help!"

as he did at the battle of Phlegra, and shot at me with all his might: he could not have joyful vengeance for it."

Then my leader spoke so forcefully that I had never heard him so loudly: "O Capaneus, in that which is not quenched

your pride, you are punished more; no torment, except your rage, would be the perfect pain for your fury."

Then he turned to me with a better face, saying: "He was one of the seven kings who besieged Thebes; and he had, and seems to have,

God in disdain, and seems to value him little; but, as I told him, his spite is a very fitting adornment for his breast.

Now come after me, and be careful not to set foot on the burning sand again; but always keep your feet close to the forest."

In silence we came to where a small stream gushes out of the forest, whose redness still shudders me.

As from the Bulicame flows a stream that the sinners then divide among themselves, so it went down through the sand.

Its bottom and both slopes were made of stone, and the edges on either side; so I realized that the pass was there.

"Among all the other things I have shown you, since we entered through the gate whose threshold is denied to no one,

nothing was seen by your eyes so notable as this present stream, which quenches all the flames above it."

These words were from my guide; whereupon I begged him to grant me the meal for which he had bestowed my desire.

"In the midst of the sea lies a wasted land," he said then, "which is called Crete, under whose king the world was once chaste.

There is a mountain there that was once blessed with water and leaves, called Ida; now it is deserted as a forbidden thing.

Rhea once chose it as her son's faithful cradle, and to better hide him, when he wept, she had him cry out there.

Within the mountain stands a great old man, his back turned toward Dammiata and Rome, gazing like a mirror.

His head is fashioned of fine gold, and his arms and chest are pure silver, then it is copper up to the fork;

from there down, it is all choice iron, except for the right foot, which is baked clay; and on that, more than on the other, he stands erect.

Every part, except the gold, is broken by a fissure that drips tears, which, collected, pierce that cave.

Their course in this valley becomes rocky; they form Acheron, Styx, and Phlegethon; then they descend through this narrow gutter,

until, where there is no further descent, they form Cocytus; and what that pool is you will see, but it is not told here."

And I to him: "If the present stream flows thus from our world, why does it only appear to us at this edge?"

And he to me: "You know that the place is round; and although you have come far, still to the left, descending to the bottom,

you have not yet circled the whole circle; therefore, if something new appears there, it should not bring wonder to your face."

And I again: "Master, where are Phlegethon and Lethe? For you are silent about the one, and the other says what is made of this rain."

"In all your questions, I certainly like you," he replied, "but the boiling of the red water should have solved one of yours.

You will see Lethe, but outside this moat, where souls go to wash when repented of their sin."

Then he said: "Now it's time to leave the woods; make sure you come after me: the edges make way, which are not burned,

and above them every vapor is extinguished."

Inferno · Canto XV

Now one of the harsh edges bears us; and the smoke of the stream above darkens, so that it protects the water and the banks from the fire.

As Flemings between Guizzante and Bruges, fearing the flood that rushes toward them, build a shield so that the sea flees;

and as Paduans along the Brenta, to defend their villas and castles, before Carentana feels the heat:

in such an image were they made, although neither so tall nor so thick, whoever he was, the master made them.

We had already moved so far from the forest that I could not have seen where he was, had I turned back,

when we encountered a group of souls coming along the bank, and each one looked at us as it is wont to look at one another at evening under a new moon; and they sharpened their brows toward us like an old tailor through the eye of a needle.

Thus, eyed by such a family, I was recognized by one who seized me by the hem and cried out: "What a marvel!"

And I, when he stretched out his arm to me, fixed my eyes on his baked face, so that his scorched face did not shield

his knowledge from my understanding; and lowering my hand to his face, I replied: "Are you here, Ser Brunetto?"

And he: "O my son, do not be displeased if Brunetto Latino goes back with you a little and lets the trail go."

I said to him: "As far as I can, I come as a prayer; and if you wish me to sit with you, I will do so, if it pleases him that I go with him."

"O son," he said, "whoever of this flock stops a moment lies there for a hundred years without being roasted when the fire strikes him.

So go on: I will come to your clothes; and then I will rejoin my band, which goes lamenting its eternal woes."

I did not dare to leave the road to walk alongside him; but I kept my head bowed like one who walks reverently.

He began: "What fortune or destiny brings you down here before the last day? And who is this who shows the way?"

"Up there, in the serene life," I replied, "I lost myself in a valley, before my years were complete.

Just yesterday morning I turned my back on it: this one appeared to me, returning to it, and brought me home by this path."

And he to me: "If you follow your star, you cannot fail to reach a glorious port, if I have well perceived it in the beautiful life;

and if I had not died so early, seeing the heavens so benign toward you, I would have given you comfort in your work.

But that ungrateful, malignant people who descended from Fiesole long ago, and still hold the mountain and the rock,

will become your enemy, for your good deeds; and rightly so, since among the jokes of sorb trees it is unfitting to bear fruit to the sweet fig.

An old report in the world calls them blind; they are a greedy, envious, and proud people: see that you cleanse yourself of their ways.

Your fortune reserves such honor for you that both sides will hunger for you; but let the grass be far from your beak.

Let the beasts of Fiesole make litter of themselves, and not touch the plant, if any still grows in their manure,

in which the holy seed of those Romans who remained there when the nest of such malice was made may live again."

"If my question were fully fulfilled," I replied, "you would not yet be banished from human nature.

For fixed in my mind is the dear and good paternal image of you, when hour after hour in the world

you taught me how man becomes eternal: and how much I deserve it, while I live, must be discerned in my language.

What you narrate of my life I write, and reserve it to be glossed in another text by a lady who will know, if I reach her.

I want it to be so clear to you, so long as my conscience does not scold me, for I am ready to serve Fortune, as she wills.

Such a pledge is not new to my ears: therefore, let Fortune turn her wheel as she pleases, and the peasant his hoe."

My master then turned his right cheek and looked back at me; then he said: "He who notes it listens well."

Nor did I speak any less with Ser Brunetto, and I asked who his most famous and greatest companions were.

And he to me: "It is good to know about some; about the others it would be praiseworthy to remain silent, for time would be short for so much exclamation.

In short, know that all were great and renowned clergymen and scholars, but are stained in the world with the same sin.

Priscian goes away with that wretched crowd, and Francesco d'Accorso too; and there, if I had had a longing for such a sting,
