I turned attentively at the first tone, and I seemed to hear "Te Deum laudamus" in a voice mingled with the sweet sound.

What I heard conveyed to me precisely such an image as one usually gets when one sits singing with organs;

for now and then one understands the words.

Purgatorio · Canto X

Then we were within the threshold of the gate that the evil love of souls disuses, because it makes the crooked path seem straight,

I heard it ringing as it was closed; and if I had turned my eyes toward it, what would have been a worthy excuse for the fault?

We were climbing through a cracked stone, which moved back and forth, like a wave that flees and approaches.

"Here it is necessary to use a little skill," my leader began, "to approach now this way, now that, the side that is departing."

And this made our steps scanty, so that before the moon's wan reached its bed to rest,

we were out of that eye of the needle; But when we were free and open up where the mountain gathers behind us,

I, tired and both of us uncertain of our path, remained on a plain more solitary than roads through deserts.

From its edge, where the void ends, to the foot of the high, rising bank, it would measure three times a human body;

and as far as my eye could stretch its wings, now from the left and now from the right flank, this cornice seemed to me like this.

Up there, our feet had not yet moved, when I recognized that bank all around, which lacked the right of ascent,

to be of white marble and adorned with carvings so that not only Polycletus but nature itself would have scorned there.

The angel who came to earth with the decree of the long-lamented peace, which opened the heavens of their long prohibition,

appeared before us there, carved in a sweet gesture so true, that he did not seem like a silent image.

One would have sworn he was saying, 'Hail!'; for there was imagined she who turned the key to unlock the lofty love;

and on her face was imprinted this phrase, "Behold, the handmaid of the Gods," just as a figure is sealed in wax.

"Do not keep your mind fixed on one place," said the sweet master, who had me on that side where the people are in my heart.

Therefore I moved my face, and saw behind Mary, on that side where he who was moving me was,

another scene set in the rock; whereupon I passed Virgil, and he drew near, so that it might be placed before my eyes.

Carved there in the marble itself was the cart and oxen, drawing the holy ark, because of which one fears an uncommitted office.

In front appeared people; and all of it, divided into seven choirs, made my two senses say, one "No," the other "Yes, sing."

Like the smoke of the incense that was imagined there, my eyes and nose became discordant with Yes and No.

There, before the blessed vase, the humble psalmist, elevated in his flirtation, was more and less than a king in that situation.

Opposite, depicted in a view of a great palace, Micol admired like a spiteful and sad woman.

I moved my feet from the place where I stood, to closely observe another story that blazed before me behind Micol.

Here was chronicled the high glory of the Roman principality, whose valor moved Gregory to his great victory;

I speak of the Emperor Trajan; and a widow was at the bridle, draped in tears and grief.

Around him it seemed crowded and crowded with knights, and the eagles in the gold above them, visible in the wind, moved.

The wretched woman among them all seemed to say: "Lord, take vengeance on my son, who is dead, for which I grieve."

And he answered her: "Now wait until I return." And she: "My lord," like one in whom grief hastens,

"if you do not return?" And he: "Whoever is where I am will do it for you." And she: "What will happen to you if you forget your own good?"

And he: "Now take heart; for it is fitting that I fulfill my duty before I move: justice demands and pity holds me back."

He who never saw anything new produced this visible speech, new to us because it is not found here.

While I delighted in gazing at the images of so many humiliations, so dear to the sight because of their creator,

"Look, over here, but their steps are rare," the poet murmured, "many people: these will lead us to the high steps."

My eyes, which were content to gaze upon the novelties for which they are eager, were not slow in turning toward him.

But I do not want, reader, that you should lose your good intentions by hearing how God wants the debt to be paid.

Do not wait for the martyr's form: think of the succession; consider that it cannot go any worse beyond the great sentence.

I began: "Master, what I see moving towards us does not seem to me to be people, and I know not what, so I am delirious at the sight."

And he to me: "The grave condition of their torment crushes them to the ground, so that my eyes first struggled with it.

But look steadily there, and unravel with your eyes what comes from beneath those rocks: now you can see how each one beats himself."

O proud Christians, wretched ones, who, weakened by the sight of your mind, trust in your backward steps,

Do you not realize that we are worms born to form the angelic butterfly, which flies to justice without shields?

Why does your soul float high, and are you almost stunned by the lack of formation, like a worm whose formation fails?

As, to support a loft or a roof, a figure is sometimes seen to join its knees to its chest,

which causes true resentment to arise in those who see it for not being true; such have I seen those made, when I took care to be careful.

True it is that they were contracted more or less according as more or less was upon them; and whoever had more patience in his gestures,

seemed to say, weeping, 'I can bear no more.'

Purgatorio · Canto XI

"O our Father, who dwells in heaven, not circumscribed, but by the greater love you have for the first effects up there,

let your name and your worth be praised by every creature, as is worthy of giving thanks to your sweet vapor.

May the peace of your kingdom come to us, for we cannot attain it ourselves, unless it comes with all our strength.

As your angels sacrifice to you for his will, singing hosannas, so may men do for his.

Give us today our daily manna, without which he who most toils to go back through this harsh desert goes.

And as we forgive everyone the evil we have suffered, so forgive, in your mercy, and do not look upon our deserts.

Our virtue, which is easily overcome, do not test it with the ancient adversary, but free it from him who so spurs it.

This last prayer, dear lord, is not made for us, for it is not necessary, but for those who remain behind us.

Thus, praying for good rebuke to themselves and us, those shades went under the burden, like what is sometimes dreamed,

all equally anguished, all round and weary, on the first ledge, purging the world's gloom.

If there is always good spoken of for us, here what can be said and done for them by those who have good roots in their will?

It is indeed their duty to wash away the notes they carry here, so that, pure and light, they may emerge to the starry wheels.

"Ah, if justice and mercy quickly disquiet you, so that you may move the wing that will lift you according to your desire,

show us on which hand the stairway leads shortest; and if there is more than one passage, show us the one that descends least steeply;

For this one who comes with me, burdened with Adam's flesh, which he wears, is reluctant to climb up, against his will.

Their words, which I returned to those spoken by the one I was following, were not from whom they came clearly;

but it was said: "Come with us on the right hand along the bank, and you will find the passage possible for a living person to ascend.

And if I were not impeded by the rock that tames my proud neck, so that I must bear my face low,

this one, who still lives and is not named, I would look, to see if I recognize him, and to make him pity this burden.

I was a Latin and born of a great Tuscan: Guglielmo Aldobrandesco was my father; I do not know if his name was ever yours.

The ancient blood and the graceful deeds of my ancestors made me so arrogant that, not thinking of our common mother,

I held every man in such contempt that I died of it, as the Sienese know, and every servant in Campagnatico knows it.

I am Omberto; and pride does not harm me alone, for it has dragged all my companions into misfortune.

And here I must bear this burden for her, so that God may be satisfied, since I did not do it among the living, here among the dead."

Listening, I bowed my face; and one of them, not he who spoke, twisted under the burden that encumbered him,

and saw me and recognized me and called out, keeping his eyes fixed with difficulty on me, who, bowed low, walked with them.

"Oh!" I said to him, "are you not Oderisi, the honor of Agobbio and the honor of that art which is called enlightenment in Paris?"

"Brother," he said, "the papers painted by Franco Bolognese laugh more; the honor is now entirely his, and partly mine.

I would not have been so courteous while I lived, for the great desire for excellence which my heart felt.

The penalty for such pride is paid here; and I would not still be here, if it were not that, having the power to sin, I turned to God.

Oh vain glory of human power! How little green remains on the summit, if it has not been reached by the great ages!

Cimabue thought he could hold the field in painting, and now Giotto has the cry, so that his fame is dark.

Thus has Guido robbed the glory of language from one another; and perhaps someone has been born who will drive both from the nest.

The worldly noise is nothing but a breath of wind, which now comes here and now comes there, and changes name because it changes side.

What voice will you have, if you cast off your old flesh, than if you were dead before you left the "pappo" and the "dindi,"

before a thousand years pass? For eternity holds a shorter space than the flicking of an eyelid in the circle that is slowest in heaven.

He who takes so little of the journey before me, all Tuscany resounded; and now in Siena he is barely whispered,

who was lord when the Florentine fury was destroyed, which was proud then as it is now depraved.

Your fame is the color of grass, which comes and goes, and he discolors it by which it emerges unripe from the earth."

And I to him: "Your true words encourage me with good humility, and you soothe my great tumor; but who is he of whom you spoke just now?"

"He is," he replied, "Provenzan Salvani; and he is here because he was presumptuous in bringing all Siena into his hands.

He has gone thus and goes, without rest, since he died; such money pays to satisfy those who are too daring down there."

And I: "If that spirit who waits, before he repents, on the edge of life, dwells down here and does not ascend up here,

if good prayer does not help him, before the time has passed as long as he lived, how was his coming granted?"

"When he lived most gloriously," he said, "freely in the Campo di Siena, all shame laid aside, he stood still;

and there, to free his friend from the pain he was enduring in Charles's prison, he brought himself to tremble in every vein.

I will say no more, and I know I speak darkly; but it will be a short time before your neighbors will see to it that you can comment on it.

This work took away those boundaries from him."

Purgatorio · Canto XII

Like oxen going under the yoke, I went with that laden soul, as long as the sweet pedagogue tolerated it.

But when he said: "Leave him and cross over; for here it is good for each one to push his own bark with wings and oars, as much as he can."

I returned to my upright position, as one must go, although my thoughts remained bowed and faint.

I had moved, and willingly followed my master's steps, and both of us already showed how light we were;

and he said to me: "Turn your eyes down: it will be good for you, to calm your path, to see the bed of your feet."

Just as, for their memory, the earthly tombs bear the inscription on the buried what they once were,

where there one often weeps for the sting of memory, which only the pious feel for their heels;

so I saw there, but of a better appearance according to the art, figured what emerges along the path outside the mountain.

I saw him who was created nobler than any other creature, descending from heaven, flashing, on one side.

I saw Briareus, pierced by the celestial veil, lying on the other side, heavy to the earth from the deadly cold.

I saw Thymbraeus, I saw Pallas and Mars, still armed, around their father, gazing at the scattered limbs of the Giants.

I saw Nimrod at the foot of his great work, almost lost, and gazing at the people who had proudly stood with him in Shinar.

O Niobe, with what sorrowful eyes I saw you marked on the road, between seven of your sons slain!

O Saul, how by your own sword you seemed dead there on Gilboa, which afterward felt neither rain nor dew!

O mad Aragône, I saw you already half a spider, sad on the rags of the work that was done for you.

O Rehoboam, your sign no longer seems to threaten here; but full of fear, it carries a chariot, without anyone else chasing it.

The hard pavement also showed how Almeon made the unfortunate ornament seem dear to his mother.

It showed how the sons threw themselves upon Sennacherib inside the temple, and how, after his death, they left him there.

It showed the ruin and the cruel carnage that Thamyris wrought when she said to Cyrus: "You have seen blood, and I will fill you with blood."

It showed how the Assyrians fled in rout after Holofernes was killed, and also the relics of the martyrdom.

It saw Troy in ashes and caves; O Ilion, how low and vile did the sign discerned there show you!

Who was a master of brush or style who could portray the shadows and lines that a subtle mind would have admired there?

The dead, the living, seemed alive: no one who saw the truth saw better than me, how far I trod, until I was bowed down.

Now be proud, and go away with haughty faces, children of Eve, and do not bow your faces so that you may see your own misfortune!

We had already passed the mountain and the sun's path far more exhausted than our unrestrained souls imagined,

when he who always awaited us ahead began: "Lift up your head; it is no longer time to wander in such suspense.

You see an angel there who is preparing to come toward us; see that the sixth handmaid is returning from the day's service.

Adorn your face and your gestures with reverence, so that you may be sent upward by delights; consider that this day will never return!"

I was so accustomed to his admonition that I did not waste time, that I could not speak to myself in silence on that matter.

The beautiful creature came to us, dressed in white, and with a face like that of a trembling morning star.

He opened his arms, and then he spread his wings; he said: "Come: here are the steps, and now the ascent is easy.

They come very rarely to this invitation: oh human race, born to fly up, why do you fall so before a light breeze?"

Menocci, where the rock was hewn; there he beat his wings on my forehead; then he promised me a safe journey.

As on the right, to ascend the mountain where sits the church that subdues the well-guided one above Rubaconte,

the bold ardour of climbing breaks down on the steps that were built at the age when the notebook and the stave were safe;

Thus the bank that falls here swiftly from the other circle eases; but here and there the high stone is sparse.

We, turning our bodies there, 'Blessed are the poor spirits!' voices sang so that no speech could tell it.

Ah, how different are those mouths from the infernal ones! For here one enters with songs, and down there with fierce laments.

We were already mounting the holy steps, and it seemed to me that I was much lighter than on the plain before me.

So I: "Master, tell me, what heavy thing has been lifted from me, that almost no toil is incurred by me in going?"

He replied: "When the Ps that still remain in your face are almost faded, as one is, completely shaved off,

your feet will be so conquered by good will that not only will they feel no fatigue, but it will be their delight to be pushed up."

Then I acted like those who walk with something on their heads unknown to them, except that signs make others suspect;

whereby the hand helps itself to ascertain, and seeks and finds, and fulfills that duty which cannot be performed by sight;

and with the fingers of my right hand, unfurled, I found just six of the letters that he who gave the keys engraved on my temples:

At which, looking, my Guide smiled.

Purgatorio · Canto XIII

We were at the top of the stairs, where the mountain recedes secondly, which, as it ascends, disheartens others.

There, a cornice surrounds the hill, like the first one, except that its arch bends more quickly.

There is no shadow there, nor any sign of its presence: the bank and the path appear clear with the livid color of the rock.

"If one waits here to ask," the poet reasoned, "I fear perhaps that our chosen one will delay too long."

Then he fixed his eyes on the sun; he made his right side center, and turned his left side.

"O sweet light, in whose trust I enter on this new path, you lead us," he said, "as one must lead here.

You warm the world, you shine upon it; unless other reason suggests otherwise, your rays should always be my guides."

As far as a thousand miles from here are counted, so far had we already gone, in a short time, through our eager desire;

and spirits were heard flying toward us, but not seen, speaking courteous invitations to the table of love.

The first voice that flew past said, "They have no wine!" aloud, and went on repeating it behind us.

And before it could be completely heard, for it was drawn out, another, "I am Orestes," passed by, crying, and still did not stop.

"Oh!" I said, "Father, what voices are these?" And as I asked, behold, the third said: "Love those from whom you have suffered harm."

And the good Master: "This strap whips the guilt of envy, and therefore the cords of the whip are drawn by love.

The rein must be of the opposite sound; I believe you will hear it, in my opinion, before you reach the pass of forgiveness.

But fix your eyes firmly on the air, and you will see people sitting before us, and each is seated beside the cave."

Then I opened my eyes more than before; I looked ahead, and I saw shadows with cloaks not unlike the color of the stone.

And after we had gone a little further, I heard cries of "Mary, now for us!"; cries of "Michael," and "Peter," and "All Saints."

I do not believe that even now there is a man so hardened on the ground who was not moved by compassion for what I saw next;

for when I had come so close to them that their gestures were clear to me, I was drained of great pain through my eyes.

They seemed to me to be covered with vile haircloth, and one supported the other with his shoulder, and all were supported by the bank.

So the blind, whose property is lost, stand at the pardons to ask for their needs, and one bows his head upon the other,

because pity is quickly felt in others, not only by the sound of the words, but by the sight that no less yearns.

And just as the sun does not reach the blind, so the light of heaven does not wish to bestow itself on the shades there, of which I now speak;

for a wire pierces and sews the eyelids of all, as it does to a wild sparrowhawk because it does not remain quiet.

It seemed to me, in going, that I was committing an outrage, seeing others without being seen; so I turned to my wise counsel.

He knew well what the mute man wanted to say; and therefore he did not wait for my question, but said: "Speak, and be brief and witty."

Virgil approached me from that side of the ledge from which one can fall, because it is garlanded by no edge;

on the other side were the devout shades, who pressed so hard through the horrible ridge that they bathed their cheeks.

I turned to them and said, "O people, confident of seeing the high light that holds your desire alone in its care,

if grace soon clears the foam of your conscience so that the river from your mind flows clearly through it,

tell me, for it will be gracious and dear to me, if there is a soul here among you that is Latin; and perhaps it will be good if I reveal it."

"O my brother, each is a citizen of a true city; but you mean that she lived as a pilgrim in Italy."

This, in reply, seemed to me to be heard somewhat further ahead than where I was standing, so I made myself heard even further away.

Among the others I saw a shadow waiting in sight; and if anyone wanted to ask, 'How?', it raised its chin like a blind man.

"Spirit," I said, "who tames you to ascend, if you are the one who answered me, tell me about yourself either by place or by name."

"I was a Sienese," he replied, "and with these others I here remit my cruel life, weeping to him who will grant it to me.

I was not wise, although I was called Sapìa, and I was far happier at the misfortunes of others than at my own fortune.

And lest you think I deceive you, hear if I was, as I tell you, mad, already descending the span of my years.

My citizens had joined the battle near Colle with their adversaries, and I prayed to God for what he willed.

Before being defeated there and turned to the bitter passages of flight; and seeing the chase, I took joy unequal to all others,

so much so that I turned my bold face upward, crying out to God: "Now I no longer fear you!", as the blackbird did at a brief calm.

I wanted peace with God at the end of my life; and yet my debt would not be abated by penance,

were it not for the fact that Pier Pettinaio remembered me in his holy prayers, who was sorry for me out of charity.

But who are you, who go asking about our conditions, and who keep your eyes loose, as I believe, and breathe reason?"

"My eyes," I said, "have still been taken from me here, but only for a short time, for the offense done by being turned with envy is small.

The fear that hangs over my soul is much greater than the torment below, which already weighs me down from below."

And she to me: "Who then brought you up here among us, if you intend to return?" And I: "This one who is with me and says nothing.

And I am alive; and therefore ask me, chosen spirit, if you want me to move my mortal feet from there for you again."

"Oh, this is such a new thing to hear," he replied, "which is a great sign that God loves you; therefore your prayer sometimes helps me.

And I ask you, for what you most desire, if ever you set foot on Tuscan soil, to glorify me well among my neighbors.

You will see them among that vain people who hope in Talamone, and they will lose more hope than in finding Diana;

but the admirals will lose more there."

Purgatorio · Canto XIV

"Who is this who circles our mountain before death has given him flight, and opens and closes his eyes at will?"

"I know not who he is, but I know he is not alone; ask him, who draws nearer, and gently, so that he may speak, receive him."

Thus two spirits, one leaning toward the other, spoke of me there on the right hand; then they turned their faces, to speak to me, supine;

and one said: "O soul, who, fixed in the body, are still going toward heaven, in charity console us and tell us

whence you come and who you are; for you make us marvel so much with your grace, as it requires something that never was before."

And I: "Through half of Tuscany runs a little river that rises in Falterona, and a hundred miles of course does not satiate it.

From above it I bring this person: to tell you who I am would be to speak in vain, for my name does not yet sound much."

"If indeed I bind your understanding with your intellect," then he who spoke first answered me, "you speak of the Arno."

And the other said to him: "Why did he hide the name of that river, just as one does of horrible things?"

And the shade who was questioned about this, thus repaid himself: "I do not know; but it is well worth losing the name of that valley;

For from its beginning, where the mountainous mountain from which Peloro is cut is so pregnant that in few places it passes beyond that mark,

to where it yields for refreshment what the sky dries up from the sea, where the rivers have what goes with them,

virtue thus flees from all as an enemy, like a snake, either through misfortune of the place, or through bad habits that search it:

whereby the inhabitants of the wretched valley have so changed their nature, that it seems as if Circe had them for pasture.

Among ugly pigs, more worthy of gall than of any other food made for human use, it first directs its poor path.

Then, coming down, it finds Botoli snarling more than it can ask of them, and turns its face away from them in disdain.

It goes falling; And the more it grows, the more dogs the cursed and unfortunate pit finds turning into wolves.

Then descending through many dark depths, it finds the foxes so full of deceit that they fear no ingenuity that can occupy them.

Nor will I fail to say why another hears me; and good will he be, if he still remembers what a true spirit unravels in me.

I see your grandson becoming a hunter of those wolves on the banks of the fierce river, and he frightens them all.

He sells their flesh while it is still alive; then he slaughters them like an ancient beast; many are deprived of life and himself of value.

Bloody, he emerges from the sad forest; leave it such that in a thousand years it will not return to its original state.

As at the announcement of painful harm the countenance of one who listens, from whatever side the danger assails him,

so I saw the other soul, who was listening, become troubled and sad, after he had gathered the words to himself.

The words of both made me want to know their names, and I asked them mingled with prayers;

whereupon the spirit who first spoke to me began again: "You want me to persuade myself to do for you what you do not want me to do.

But since God wants his grace to shine so brightly in you, I will not be stingy to you; therefore, know that I was Guido del Duca.

My blood was so burned with envy that if it had seen a man become happy, you would have seen me spilled with hatred.

From my seed I reap such straw; oh human race, why do you place your heart where there is no need for a forbidden consort?

This is Rinier; this is the value and honor of the house of Calboli, where no one has since inherited its worth.

And not only is his blood barren, between the Po and the mountains and the sea and the Rhine, of the good required for truth and amusement;

for within these borders it is filled with poisonous undergrowth, so that it would be too late to cultivate it now.

Where is the good Lizio and Arrigo Mainardi?
Pierre Traversaro and Guido di Carpigna?
Oh Romagnuoli turned bastards!

When in Bologna does a blacksmith rejoice? When in Faenza a Bernardin di Fosco, a gentle wand of small weed?

Do not be surprised if I weep, Tuscan, when I remember, with Guido da Prata, Ugolin d'Azzo who lived with us,

Federigo Tignoso and his company, the Traversara household and the Anastagi (and both are directed),

the ladies and gentlemen, the troubles and comforts that love and courtesy inspired in us, where hearts are made so wicked.

O Bretinoro, why do you not flee, since your family and many others have left to avoid being wicked?

Bagnacaval does well, for he does not re-offend; and Castrocaro does badly, and Conio worse, for he is still entangled in siring such counts.

The Pagans will do well, since the devil will turn away from them; but not because no pure witness will ever remain of them.

O Ugolin de' Fantolin, your name is safe, since no one is expected to darken it by degenerating.

But go away, Tuscan, now; for now I delight in weeping more than in speaking, so our reason has constrained my mind.

We knew that those dear souls heard us go; therefore, by remaining silent, they made us confident about the journey.

Then we were alone, walking forward. A voice seemed like a flash of lightning when it splits the air, and it came from opposite, saying:

"Whoever apprehends me will kill me." And it fled like thunder that fades away if the cloud suddenly descends.

As soon as our hearing had ceased, the other came with such a great din that it resembled a thunder that follows quickly:

"I am Aglauros who became stone." And then, to draw closer to the poet, I took a step to the right, and not forward.

The breeze was already quiet on all sides; and he said to me: "That was the hard crag that man should keep within his goal.

But you take the bait, so that the hook of the ancient adversary draws you to himself; and therefore bridle or recall is of little avail.

You called to heaven, and it revolves around you, showing you its eternal beauties, and your eye only gazes at the earth;

where he who discerns all things strikes you.

Purgatorio · Canto XV

As much as between the end of the third hour and the beginning of the day seems the sphere that always plays like a child,

so much so did the sun already seem to have left its course toward evening; there it was vespers, and here it was midnight.

And the rays struck us in the middle of our noses, because the mountain had revolved so much for us that we were already going straight toward the sunset,

when I felt my forehead weighed down by the splendor much more than before, and I was amazed at the unknown things;

whereupon I raised my hands to the tops of my lashes and made myself a sunshade, which smooths away the excess of visibility.

As when a ray leaps from water or a mirror to the opposite side, rising in a different direction

to that which descends, and departs so far from the falling stone in equal measure, as experience and art demonstrate;

so it seemed to me that I was struck by refracted light there before me; wherefore my sight was forced to flee.

"What is that, sweet father, why I cannot shield my face enough to avail me," I said, "and yet it seems to be moving toward us?"

"Do not marvel if the family of heaven still dazzles you," he replied: "a messenger is coming to bid someone ascend."

Soon it will be that seeing these things will not be a burden to you, but a delight to you, as nature disposed you to feel."

Then we reached the blessed angel, who said in a joyful voice: "Enter here a stairway less steep than the others."

We were climbing, already separated from the lynxes, and "Blessed are the merciful!" was sung behind, and "Rejoice, you who conquer!"

My master and I, both alone, were going up; and I thought, as I went, I could gain something from his words;

and I turned to him, asking: "What did the spirit of Romagna mean by mentioning 'prohibition' and 'consort'?"

To which he said to me: "He knows the harm of his greater misfortune; and therefore he should not be surprised, he should blame himself, so that he may weep less.

Because your desires are fixed where, through company, a part is diminished, envy moves the mantaco to your sighs.

But if the love of the supreme sphere were to twist your desire upward, that fear would not be in your breast;

for, the more one calls himself 'ours,' the more good each possesses, and more charity burns in that cloister."

"I am more hungry for contentment," I said, "than if I had previously kept silent, and I gather more doubt in my mind.

How can it be that a good, distributed among many possessors, makes one richer in itself than if it is possessed by few?"

And he to me: "Because you fix your mind only on earthly things, you dispel the darkness of true light.

That infinite and ineffable good that is up there runs to love as a ray comes to a shining body.

It gives as much as it finds of ardor; so that, as charity extends, the eternal value grows within it.

And the more people there are, the more good there is to love, and the more they love, and like a mirror they reflect one another.

And if my reasoning does not satiate you, you will see Beatrice, and she will completely take away from you this and every other desire.

Just see to it that the five wounds, which are healing from grief, are soon extinguished, as the two already are.

As I wanted to say, "You satisfy me," I saw myself arrive at the other circle, so that the lingering lights made me silent.

There I seemed to be suddenly drawn into an ecstatic vision and see many people in a temple;

and a woman, upon entering, said with a mother's sweet gesture: "My son, why have you acted thus toward us?

Look, your father and I were grieving and seeking you." And as soon as I was silent here, what had seemed before vanished.

Then another appeared to me, with those waters running down her cheeks that grief distils when it arises from great scorn in others,

and said: "If you are lord of the village over whose name there was such strife among the gods, and from which all knowledge sparkles,

avenge yourself on those bold arms that embraced our daughter, O Pisistratus." And the lord seemed to me, benign and gentle,

to answer her with a calm expression: "What shall we do to those who desire us harm, if he who loves us is condemned for us?"

Then I saw people inflamed with rage kill a young man with stones, crying out loudly to themselves: "Martyrdom, martyrdom!"

And I saw him bending toward the earth, because of the death that already weighed him down, but his eyes always made gates to heaven,

praying to the high Lord, in such great war, that he would forgive his persecutors, with that look that releases pity.

When my soul returned from outside to the things that are true outside of it, I recognized my true errors.

My guide, who could see me acting like one who is unbound by sleep, said: "What is the matter that you cannot restrain yourself?

But have you come more than half a league, veiling your eyes and with your legs wrapped around you, as if wine or sleep were subduing you?"

"O my sweet father, if you will listen to me, I will tell you," I said, "what appeared to me when my legs were thus taken from me."

And he: "If you had a hundred ghosts on your face, your thoughts would not be closed to me, however much they seemed.

What you saw was because you had no excuse for opening your heart to the waters of peace that are diffused from the eternal source.

I did not ask 'What ails you?' because of what one does who only looks with an unseeing eye when the body lies lifeless;

but I asked to give strength to your feet: thus should the lazy search, slow to use their vigilance when it returns."

We were walking through the evening, attentive beyond our eyes' reach against the bright evening rays.

And behold, little by little, a smoke was gathering toward us, like dark night; and there was no place to turn away from it.

This took away our eyes and the pure air.

Purgatorio · Canto XVI

The darkness of hell and of night stripped of every planet, beneath a poor sky, as darkened as can be by cloud,

did not cast such a thick veil over my face as the smoke that covered us there, nor did the feeling of such harshness,

that my eyes could not stand to stay open; whereupon my wise and faithful escort approached me and offered me his shoulder.

As a blind man follows his guide so as not to lose his way and not to collide with something that will disturb him, or perhaps kill him,

I went through the bitter and filthy air, listening to my leader who said only: "See that you are not cut off from me."

I heard voices, and each seemed to be praying for peace and mercy to the Lamb of God who takes away sins.

Only "Agnus Dei" were their exordia; There was one word in all, and one tone, so that all concord seemed among them.

"Are those spirits, Master, that I hear?" I said. And he to me: "You truly understand, and they are unraveling the knot of wrath."

"Now who are you, who rent our smoke, and speak of us only as if you still divided time for yours by the calends?"

Thus it was spoken by one voice; whereupon my Master said: "Answer, and ask if one goes up from there."

And I: "O creature who purifies yourself to return beautiful to him who made you, you will hear a marvel if you follow me."

"I will follow you as far as I am permitted," he replied; "and if seeing smoke does not allow us, hearing will keep us united instead."

Then I began: "With that swaddling band that death unties, I go upward, and I came here through the infernal anguish.

And if God has enclosed me in his grace, so that he wants me to see his court in a manner entirely different from modern custom,

do not hide from me who you were before death, but tell me, and tell me if I am well on my way; and your words will be our escorts."

"I was a Lombard, and I was called Marco; I knew of the world, and I loved that valor to which everyone has now drawn his bow.

Go straight to mount upward." Thus he answered, and added: "I beg you to pray for me when you are up there."

And I to him: "By faith I bind myself to you to do what you ask of me; but I burst into doubt if I do not explain it to myself.

First it was simple, and now it is doubled in your meaning, which makes me certain here, and elsewhere, that to which I couple it.

The world is indeed as completely deserted of every virtue as you are to me, and pregnant and covered with malice;

but I pray that you point out to me the cause, so that I may see it and show it to others; for one in heaven, and one below, places it."

He first let out a deep sigh, which constricted grief in "Alas!"; and then he began: "Brother, the world is blind, and you indeed come from it.

You who live, bring every cause up to heaven, as if everything moved by necessity.

If this were so, free will would be destroyed in you, and there would be no justice for joy in good, and mourning for evil.

Heaven initiates your movements; I do not say all of them, but, granting that I say so, light is given to you for good and evil,

and free will; which, if it endures hardship in the first battles with heaven, then conquers all, if it is well nourished.

You are free to submit to greater force and a better nature; and that is created in you by the mind, which heaven does not care for.

Therefore, if the present world goes astray, the cause is in you, in you it is asked; and I will now be your true spy.

It slips from the grasp of him who longs for it before it exists, like a girl who weeps and laughs,

the simple soul that knows nothing, except that, moved by a happy Maker, it willingly returns to that which amuses it.

It first savors a small good; there it deceives itself, and runs after it, without guidance or restraint to turn its love away.

Therefore, a law was needed to restrain; a king was needed who could discern at least the tower of the true city.

The laws exist, but who lays hands on them? No one, because the shepherd who proceeds can rustle, but his hooves are not cloven;

Therefore, the people, whom their guide sees striking only at that good for which they are greedy, feed on that, and ask no more.

You can see clearly that bad conduct is the cause that has made the world evil, and not nature that is corrupt in you.

Rome, which created the good world, used to have two suns, which showed one and the other path, both of the world and of God.

One has destroyed the other; and the sword has joined the crook, and one must not go together with the other by force;

because, once united, one does not fear the other: if you do not believe me, consider the ear of corn, for every herb is known by its seed.

In the land that the Adige and Po rivers flow, valor and courtesy used to be found, before Frederick had trouble;

now one can safely pass by anyone who left out, out of shame to converse with good men or to approach them.

Indeed, there are still three old men in whom the old age reproaches the new, and it seems to them that God will restore them to a better life:

Currado da Palazzo, the good Gherardo, and Guido da Castel, who is better known, in French, as the simple Lombard.

Say now that the Church of Rome, by confusing two regiments, falls into the mire, both disgracing itself and its burden.

"O my Marco," I said, "you argue well; and now I discern why the sons of Levi were excluded from the inheritance.

But what Gherardo is he whom you say, as a sample, is left of the extinct race, in reproach of the savage age?"

"Either your speech deceives me, or it tempts me," he replied; "for, speaking Tuscan, it seems you know nothing of the good Gherardo.

I know him by no other nickname, unless I took it from his daughter Gaia. God be with you, for I come no more with you.

You see the dawn already shining white through the smoke, and I must leave (the angel is there) before I appear to him."

So he returned, and he would hear me no more.

Purgatorio · Canto XVII

Remember, reader, if ever a fog overtook you in the Alps, through which you could see no differently than through the skin of a mole,

how, when the humid and thick vapors begin to disperse, the sun's sphere feebly enters through them;

and your imagination will be light in reaching to see how I once again saw the sun, which was already setting.

Yes, matching my steps with the faithful steps of my master, I emerged from that cloud to the rays already dead on the lower shores.

O imagination that sometimes steals us so far from the outside that one does not notice why a thousand trumpets sound around,

who moves you, if sense does not offer you? Move, light that takes shape in the sky, by itself or by the will that sees it below.

Of her wickedness, which changed form into the bird that most delights in singing, the trace appeared in my imagination;

and here my mind was so closed within itself that nothing came from outside that it could then shelter.

Then a crucified man, spiteful and fierce in his sight, rained down upon my lofty imagination, and thus he died;

around him were the great Axerus, his wife Esther, and righteous Mordecai, who was so perfect in his words and deeds.

And as this image burst by itself, like a bubble lacking the water it had formed,

a girl arose in my vision, weeping bitterly, and saying: "O queen, why did you choose to be nothing out of anger?

You have Ancisa so as not to lose Lavina; now you have lost me!" I am she who mourns, mother, before your ruin comes to another's.

As sleep is shattered when suddenly a new light strikes the closed face, which, broken, flickers before it dies entirely;

so my imagination fell as soon as a light struck my face, far greater than our usual light.

I turned to see where I was, when a voice said, "Here we go up," which drew me from all other intents;

and made my desire so eager to see who it was who was speaking that it never rests unless confronted.

But as before the sun that burdens our sight and veils its form by excessive brightness, so my virtue failed there.

"This is a divine spirit, who directs us on the path to ascend without entreaty, and with its light conceals itself.

It is with us, as a man makes himself fat; for he who waits for a prayer and sees the need, already maliciously sets himself to denial.

Now let us grant our feet to such an invitation; let us try to climb before it darkens, for then we will not be able to, if the day does not return."

Thus said my leader, and I with him turned our steps to a stairway; and as soon as I was on the first step,

I felt near me almost a fluttering of wings, fanning my face and saying: "Blessed are the peacemakers, who are free from evil anger!"

The last rays that follow the night were already so high above us that the stars appeared on many sides.

"O my virtue, why do you vanish so?" I said to myself, for I felt the strength of my legs placed in truce.

We were where the ladder no longer ascended, and we were stationary, just like a ship arriving at the shore.

And I waited a little, to see if I heard anything in the new circle; then I turned to my Master and said:

"Sweet Father, tell me, what offense is being purged here in the circle where we are? If our feet remain still, let there be no speech from you."

And he to me: "The love of good, bereft of its duty, is restored here; here the ill-delayed oar is beaten back.

But so that you may understand more clearly, turn your mind to me, and you will reap some good fruit from our stay."

"Neither creator nor creature," he began, "my son, was ever without love, whether natural or of the soul; and you know it.

Nature is always without error, but the other can err through an evil object or through too much or too little vigor.

As long as it is well-directed in the first, and measures itself in the second, it cannot be the cause of evil delight;

but when it turns toward evil, either with more or less care than it should, it runs toward good, and its creation must go against its Maker.

From this you can understand that love must be the seed in you of every virtue and every action that merits pain.

Now, because love can never turn its gaze from the salvation of its subject, all things are hated;

and because one cannot understand anything divided, and standing alone, anything is from the first, and every effect is determined by it to hate.

It remains, if I judge rightly by dividing, that the evil one loves belongs to one's neighbor; And this love arises in three ways in your mire.

There is one who, because his neighbor is eliminated, hopes for excellence, and for this reason alone desires that he be debased from his greatness;

there is one who fears losing power, grace, honor, and fame because others will rise above them, and so saddens him that he loves the opposite;

and there is one who seems to be shamed by injury, so that he becomes greedy for revenge, and must be such as to inflict evil on others.

This threefold love down here is lamented: now I want you to understand the other, which runs toward good in a corrupted order.

Each one confusedly apprehends a good in which the soul is calmed, and desires; therefore each strives to attain it.

If slow love draws you to see it or to acquire it, this frame, after just repentance, torments you.

There is another good that does not make man happy; it is not happiness, it is not the good essence, the fruit and root of every good.

The love that abandons itself too much to it is lamented above us in three circles; but since it is spoken of in three parts,

I am silent, so that you may seek it for yourself.

Purgatorio · Canto XVIII

The lofty teacher had concluded his discourse, and was attentively looking into my face to see if I seemed content;

And I, still tormented by a new thirst, remained silent on the outside, and said within: "Perhaps my asking too much is burdensome."

But that true father, who noticed the timid desire that was not opening, speaking, gave me the courage to speak.

Whereupon I: "Master, my vision is so enlivened by your light that I discern clearly what your reasoning departs or describes.

Therefore I pray you, sweet, dear father, to show me love, to which you refer every good deed and its opposite."

"Direct," he said, "toward me the keen lights of your intellect, and make manifest the error of the blind who make themselves leaders.

The soul, which is created to love readily, is moved to everything that pleases, as soon as it is awakened by pleasure in action.

Your apprehension draws intention from truth, and unfolds it within you, so that your soul turns to it;

and if, turned, it inclines toward it, that inclination is love, that is nature that binds itself anew within you for pleasure.

Then, just as fire moves upward because of its form, which was born to ascend where its matter endures most,

so the seized soul enters into desire, which is a spiritual motion, and never rests until the thing loved makes it rejoice.

Now it may appear to you how hidden truth is from those who consider each love in itself a praiseworthy thing;

Because perhaps its material always appears to be good, but not every sign is good, even if the wax is good."

"Your words and my faithful mind," I replied to him, "have revealed love to me, but this has made me more pregnant with doubt;

for if love is offered to us from without and the soul does not walk with another foot, whether it goes straight or crooked, it is not its merit."

And he to me: "What reason sees here, I can tell you; from then on, wait only for Beatrice, who is a work of faith.

Every substantial form, separated from matter and united with it, has within itself a specific virtue,

which without action is not felt, nor is ever demonstrated except through effect, as life is through green leaves on a plant.

But man does not know where the intellect of the first knowledge comes from, nor does affection of the first desirable things,

which are in you like the bee's eagerness to make honey; and this first desire does not understand the merit of praise or blame.

Now, so that all others may be gathered to this, there is innate virtue that counsels, and it must hold the threshold of assent.

This is the principle from which reason for merit is taken in you, according as it welcomes and sifts good and bad loves.

Those who, reasoning, went to the bottom, became aware of this innate freedom; therefore they left morality to the world.

Therefore, let us suppose that every love that is kindled within you arises from necessity, you have the power to restrain it.

The noble virtue of Beatrice understands free will, and therefore be sure to keep it in mind if you dare speak.

The moon, almost late at midnight, made the stars appear more sparse to us, like a still-burning bucket;

and it raced against the sky along those roads that the sun inflames when he from Rome between Sardinia and Corsica sees it when it sets.

And that gentle shade for which Pietola is named more than a Mantuan village had laid aside the burden of my burden;

wherefore I, who had gathered reason open and level over my questions, stood like one who is drowsy in vain.

But this drowsiness was suddenly roused from me by people who had already turned toward us after our backs.

And as Ismenus once saw, and Asopus far away by night, the fury and throng, if only the Thebans needed Bacchus,

so he scurries through that circle of his, from what I saw of those coming, whose good will and just love ride.

He was soon upon us, for the whole great crowd was moving at a run; and two in front were crying out:

"Maria ran in haste to the mountain; and Caesar, to subdue Ilerda, stung Marseilles and then ran to Spain."

“Quickly, quickly, let time not be wasted for little love,” the others shouted after, “so that the study of doing well may renew grace.”

«O people in whom keen fervor now perhaps compensates for negligence and delay on your part due to tepidity in doing well,

he who lives, and I am certainly not lying to you, wants to go up, as long as the sun shines on him; but tell me where it is near the hole."

These were the words of my duke; and one of those spirits said: «Come behind us, and you will find the hole.

We are so eager to move that we cannot stay; but forgive, if our justice is rude, keep it.

I was abbot in San Zeno in Verona under the rule of the good Barbarossa, of whom Milan still speaks painfully.

And he already has one foot in the grave, who will soon mourn that monastery, and will be sad to have had the power;

because his son, ill of the whole body, and of the mind worse, and who was born ill, has placed in the place of his true shepherd."

I don't know if he said more or if he was silent, he had already passed beyond us; but this I understood, and it pleased me to think.

And he who was my help for every purpose said: "Turn here: see two of them coming and giving a bite to sloth."

Behind them all they said: «First the people for whom the sea parted died, before Jordan saw his heirs.

And she who did not suffer the trouble until the end with the son of Anchises, offered herself to a life without glory."

Then when those shadows separated from us so much that we could no longer see each other, a new thought arose within me,

of which many others were born and different; and I raved so much about one thing after another that I covered my eyes out of loveliness,

and I transformed the thought into a dream.

Purgatorio · Canto XIX

In the hour when the heat of the day can no longer cool the cold of the moon, overcome by the earth, and sometimes by Saturn

—when the geomancers see their Greater Fortune in the East, before the dawn, it will arise because it is little dark—,

There came to me in a dream a stammering female, squinty in her eyes, and distorted above her feet, with maimed hands, and of a pale colour.

I looked at her; and as the sun comforts the cold limbs that the night aggravates, so my gaze took care of them

his tongue, and then he straightened it all in a little while, and colored his lost face, as love would have it.

Then, when her speech was so free, she began to sing so much that with pain I would have turned my attention away from her.

«I am», he sang, «I am sweet serene, who disenchants sailors in the middle of the sea; I am so pleased to feel full!

I turned Ulysses on his wandering path to my side; and what is used with me, they rarely leave; yes I fully satisfy it!».

Her mouth was not yet closed, when a woman appeared holy and took great pains to make her confused.

«O Virgil, Virgil, who is this?», he proudly said; and he came with his eyes fixed even on the honest one.

He took the other one, and opened it in front, splitting the drapes, and showed me his belly; that woke me up with the stench coming out of it.

I moved my eyes, and the good master: «At least three voices I have given you!», said, «Rise up and come; let us find the opening through which you enter."

Up I rose, and all the circles of the sacred mountain were already filled with the high day, and we went with the new sun on our backs.

Following him, he carried my forehead like one who is full of thoughts, who makes of himself half an arch of a bridge;

when I heard “Come; here we cross" to speak in a sweet and benign way, such as is not heard in this mortal brand.

With outstretched wings, which seemed to be those of a swan, he who spoke to us thus between two walls of the hard rock turned us upward.

Then he moved his feathers and fanned us, declaring, "Here flow," that blessed are those who will have to console women's souls.

"What ails you, that you only gaze toward the earth?" my guide began to say to me, both of us a little above the angel.

And I: "A new vision makes me go with such suspicion that it bends me to itself, so that I cannot depart from the thought."

"You saw," he said, "that ancient witch who alone above us is now lamented; you saw how man frees himself from her.

Enough of yourself, and stamp your heels; turn your eyes to the worn-out one who turns the eternal king with his great wheels."

Like the falcon, which first gazes at its feet, then turns at the cry and stretches out for the longing for the food that draws it there,

such I became; and such, as the rock splits to give way to one who goes upward, I went as far as the circle begins.

When I was opened on the fifth circle, I saw people weeping along it, lying on the ground all turned downward.

"Adhaesit pavement, my soul!" I heard them say with such deep sighs that the words could barely be understood.

"O elect of God, whose sufferings justice and hope make less harsh, direct us toward the high ascents."

"If you come from lying secure, and wish to find the way more quickly, let your right hands always be outside."

Thus prayed the poet, and a little before us he answered thus: For in speaking I perceived the other hidden,

and turned my eyes to the eyes of my lord: whereupon he assented with a joyful nod to what the sight of desire demanded.

When I could do with myself as I pleased, I drew myself to that creature whose words had first made me note,

saying: "Spirit in whom weeping ripens that without which one cannot return to God, pause a little for me, your greater care.

Tell me who you were and why you have turned your backs upward, and if you wish me to obtain for you something from beyond whence I came in life."

And he to me: "Why heaven turns our backs to itself, you will know; but first know what I was, Petri's successor.

Between Siestri and Chiaveri a beautiful river flows, and the title of my blood makes its headwaters.

A little more than a month I experienced how heavy a mantle weighs on those who guard it from the mud, how all other burdens seem like feathers.

My conversion, alas!, was slow; but, as I became a Roman shepherd, so I discovered the deceitful life.

I saw that there my heart was not at peace, nor could I ascend further in that life; for which love for it was kindled within me.

Up until that point I was a miserable soul separated from God, completely avaricious; now, as you see, here I am punished for it.

What avarice does is here revealed in the purgation of converted souls; and no punishment does the mountain have more bitter.

Just as our eye never rose above, fixed on earthly things, so justice here cast it down to earth.

As avarice extinguished our love for every good, whereby we were lost in action, so justice holds us here bound,

bound and imprisoned in our feet and hands; and as long as the just Lord pleases, so long will we remain motionless and stretched out."

I was kneeling and was about to speak; but as I began, and he, just by listening, became aware of my reverence,

"What reason," he said, "has turned you down thus?" And I to him: "For your dignity, my conscience has remorse."

"Straighten your legs, rise up, brother!" he replied; "do not err: I am with you and the others in one power.

If you ever heard that holy evangelical sound that says 'Neque nubent', you can see well why I speak thus.

Go away now: I don't want you to stay any longer; for your room is a nuisance to me, and with it I mature what you said.

I have a niece there whose name is Alagia, good in herself, provided our house doesn't make her an example of evil;

and this alone is left to me there.

Purgatorio · Canto XX

Against a better will, a will fights ill; therefore, against my pleasure, to please it, I drew the sponge from the insatiable water.

We set out; and my guide moved through the steep places, along the rock, as one goes along a wall narrow to the battlements;

For the people who, drop by drop, pour through their eyes the evil that occupies the whole world, approach too closely from the other side.

Cursed be you, ancient she-wolf, who more than all other beasts have preyed because of your endless, dark hunger!

O heaven, in whose revolutions one seems to believe the conditions down here are transformed, when will there come through whom this one descends?

We walked with slow and scanty steps, and I attentively watched the shades, whom I heard piteously weeping and lamenting;

and by chance I heard "Sweet Mary!" before us, called out in tears, as a woman in childbirth;

and continued: "You were as poor as can be seen in that hospice where you laid out your sacred burden."

Next I heard: "O good Fabrizio, you wanted virtue through poverty rather than great wealth through vice."

These words had pleased me so much that I withdrew to gain knowledge of the spirit from which they seemed to have come.

He was still speaking of the generosity that Nicholas showed to the young women, to raise their youth to honor.

"O soul who speaks so well, tell me who you were," I said, "and why you alone renew these worthy praises.

Your word will not be without mercy, if I return to complete the short journey of that life that flies to its end."

And he: "I will tell you, not for the comfort I await from there, but because so much grace shines in you before you are dead.

I was the root of the evil plant that darkens all Christian land, so that good fruit is rarely plucked from it.

But if Doagio, Lilla, Guanto, and Bruggia could, vengeance would soon be taken; and I ask it of him who overpowers all.

I was called from there Ugo Ciappetta; From me were born the Philips and Louises, by whom France is newly governed.

I was the son of a butcher of Paris: when all the ancient kings passed away, except one in gray robes,

I found the reins of the kingdom's government tightened in my hands, and so powerful was my power for new acquisitions, and so full of friends,

that the head of my son was promoted to the widowed crown, from whom their sacred bones should begin.

While the great Provençal dowry did not remove the shame from my blood, it was of little avail, but still it did no harm.

There he began his plunder with force and lies; and then, as amends, he took Ponts, Normandy, and Gascony.

Charles came to Italy and, as amends, made the victim of Conradino; and then, as amends, he thrust Thomas back to heaven.

I see the time, not long after this, for another Charles to lead out of France, to make himself and his followers better known.

He comes out unarmed, and only with the lance with which Judas jousted, and he thrusts it so that it makes Florence burst.

Therefore, not land, but sin and shame will he gain, all the more grievous for himself the more trivial such damage is considered.

The other, who has already left the ship captured, I see selling his daughter and bargaining for her as corsairs do with other slaves.

O greed, what more can you do, since you have drawn my blood to you so that it cares not for its own flesh?

To make the future evil and the past seem less apparent, I see the fleur-de-lis enter Alagna, and Christ is captured in its vicar.

I see him mocked once again; I see the vinegar and gall rekindled, and he is killed among living thieves.

I see the new Pilate so cruel that this does not satiate him, but without a decree he carries his greedy sails into the Temple.

O my Lord, when will I be happy to see the vengeance that, hidden, sweetens your anger in your secret?

What I said of that only spouse of the Holy Spirit, and which made you turn to me for some commentary,

is answered to all our prayers as long as the day lasts; but as it grows dark, we take the opposite sound in her stead.

We repeat Pygmalion then, whose lust for gold made him a traitor, a thief, and a murderer;

and the misery of greedy Midas, which followed his greedy demand, which is always worth laughing at.

Everyone then remembers mad Achan, how he stole the spoils, so that the wrath of Joshua still seems to bite him.

Then we accuse Sapphira and her husband; we praise the kicks Heliodorus received; and the whole mountain revolves in infamy

Polynestor, who killed Polydorus; finally they cry out to us: "Tell Crassus, you know: what tastes like gold?"

Sometimes one speaks loudly, the other softly, according to the affection that spurs us now to greater and now to lesser pace:

Therefore, for the good that is spoken of all day, I was not alone before; but here nearby no one raised his voice.

We had already departed from it, and were struggling to overcome the road as far as our power permitted,

when I felt the mountain tremble like something falling; whereupon a chill gripped me, such as usually grips one who is going to death.

Truly, Delos never shook so violently before Latona made her nest in it to give birth to the two eyes of heaven.

Then a cry began from all sides, such that the Master turned toward me, saying: "Do not hesitate, while I lead you."

"Glory to the Most High!" they all cried, "God!" as far as I could tell from those nearby, so that I could understand the cry.

We stood still and in suspense, like the shepherds who first heard that song, until the trembling ceased and it was complete.

Then we resumed our holy journey, gazing at the shades lying on the ground, already returned to their accustomed weeping.

No ignorance ever made me so eager to know, if my memory does not err in this,

as I seemed to have then, thinking; neither in haste did I dare ask, nor could I see anything there:

so I went, timid and thoughtful.

Purgatorio · Canto XXI

The natural thirst, never sated except by the water for which the Samaritan woman begged,

tormented me, and my haste drove me along the awkward path following my leader, and I grieved for just vengeance.

And behold, just as Luke writes that Christ appeared to the two who were on the road, already risen from the sepulchral hole,

a shadow appeared to us, and it came behind us, watching from below the reclining crowd. We did not notice it, for it first spoke,

saying: "O my brothers, may God give you peace." We turned quickly, and Virgil gave us the appropriate sign.

Then he began: "In the blessed council may the true court that relegates me to eternal exile put you at peace."

"What!" he said, and we were moving forward: "if you are shades whom God does not deign to ascend, who has so carefully guided you along his ladder?"

And my teacher: "If you consider the signs that this one bears and that the angel outlines, you will see that he must reign with good.

But because she who spins day and night had not yet drawn the distaff that Clotho imposes on each and compiles,

her soul, which is your sister and mine, coming up, could not come alone, because it does not see in our way.

Therefore I was drawn from the wide throat of hell to show him, and I will show him further, as far as my school can lead him.

But tell me, if you know, why the mountain gave such collapses just now, and why all at once it seemed to cry out even to its tender feet."

So he gave me, asking, through the eye of my desire, that even with hope my thirst became less quenched.

He began: "There is nothing that the mountain religion feels without order, or that is out of custom.

Here it is free from all change: what the heaven receives from itself can be the cause, and nothing else.

Therefore, no rain, no rain, no snow, no dew, no frost falls higher than a short stairway of three steps;

clouds do not appear thick, nor sparse, nor glittering, nor daughter of Thaumas, who often changes direction there;

dry vapor does not rise higher than at the summit of the three steps I spoke of, where Peter's vicar has his feet.

Perhaps it trembles a little or a lot lower down; but for a wind that hides on earth, I know not how, it has never trembled up here.

We tremble when some pure soul feels itself, so that it rises or moves to climb upward; and this cry follows.

Only the will to purify itself proves its worth, which, completely free to change convents, surprises the soul, and its will benefits it.

First it wills, indeed, but it does not abandon the desire that divine justice, contrary to its will, as it did to sin, puts to torment.

And I, who have lain in this pain for five hundred years and more, only now felt free will to a better threshold:

Therefore you heard the tremor, and the pious spirits across the mountain give praise to that Lord, who soon sends them up."

So he said to us; and since one enjoys drinking as much as the thirst is great, I cannot say how brave it made me.

And the wise guide: "Now I see the net that entangles you here and how it unravels, why it trembles and what you rejoice at.

Now who you were, may it please you that I know, and why you have lain here for so many centuries, may your words tell me."

"At the time when good Titus, with the help of the supreme king, avenged the wounds from which the blood flowed for the vengeance of Judas,

with the name that endures most and is most honored, I was there," that spirit replied, "famous indeed, but not yet with faith.

So sweet was my vocal spirit that, a Toulouse native, Rome drew me to itself, where I deserved to adorn my temples with myrtle.

Statius the people there still call me: I sang of Thebes, and then of great Achilles; but I fell on the road with the second burden.

To my ardor were seeded the sparks, which warm me, of the divine flame from which more than a thousand are lit;

I speak of the Aeneid, which was my mother, and my nurse, in making poetry: without it I did not bear the weight of a dram.

And having lived there when Virgil lived, I would consent to a sun more than I ought to at my exit from exile.

Virgil turned these words to me with a face that, silent, said, "Be silent." But the power that wills cannot do everything;

for laughter and tears are so much a follower of the passion from which each one is born, that they follow less the will of the most truthful.

I, however, smiled like a man who winks; whereupon the shadow fell silent, and he looked into my eyes, where the countenance is most fixed;

and "If you consummate so much labor for good," he said, "why did your face just now show me a flash of laughter?"

Now I am torn between one side and the other: one makes me silent, the other begs me to speak; whereupon I sigh, and am heard

by my Master, and "Do not be afraid," he says to me, "to speak; but speak and tell him what he asks with such care."

Whereupon I said: "Perhaps you marvel, ancient spirit, at the laughter I gave; but I want you to be filled with more wonder.

This one who guides my eyes on high is that Virgil from whom you learned to sing loudly of men and gods.

If you believed any other reason for my laughter, abandon it as untrue, and believe those words you spoke of him to be true."

He was already bending to embrace the feet of my Master, but he said to him: "Brother, do not do so, for you are a shadow and you see a shadow."

And he, rising, said: "Now you can comprehend the quantity of the love that warms me for you, when I forget our vanity,

treating the shadows as something solid."

Purgatorio · Canto XXII

The angel had already remained behind us, the angel who had turned us to the sixth circle, having shorn a stroke from my face;

and he had called those who had their desire for justice blessed, and his words with "sitiunt," without anything else, fulfilled this.

And I went more lightly than through the other passages, so that without any labor I followed the swift spirits upward;

when Virgil began: "Love, inflamed with virtue, always kindled another, so long as his flame appeared outside;

So, from the moment Juvenal descended among us into the limbo of hell, who made your affection known to me,

my love for you was more than ever bound by an unseen person, so that now these stairs seem short to me.

But tell me, and as a friend forgive me if too much security loosens my reins, and as a friend now reason with me:

How could avarice find room within your breast, amidst so much wisdom that you were filled with through your care?"

These words first made Statius laugh a little; then he replied: "Every word you say of love is a dear sign to me.

Truly, things often appear that give false material for doubt because of the true reasons that are hidden.

Your question confirms to me that I was greedy in the afterlife, perhaps because of the circle where I was.

Now know that greed was too much in me, and this excess has been punished by thousands of lunatics.

And if it were not that I directed my attention when I heard you cry out, almost angered by human nature:

'Why do you not control, O sacred hunger for gold, the appetite of mortals?', turning, I would hear the grim jousts.

Then I realized that my hands could spread their wings too far to spend, and I repented of that as well as of other evils.

How many will rise again with hair shorn through ignorance, which, living in extremity, robs them of repentance for this sin!

And know that the guilt that repels any sin by direct opposition dries up here along with it;

therefore, if I have been among those people who lament avarice, to purge myself, I have met its opposite.

"Now when you sang the cruel weapons of Jocasta's double sadness," said the singer of the buccolic poems,

"from what Cléo feels with you there, it does not seem that faith still made you faithful, without which good deeds are not enough.

If so, what sun or what candles darkened you so that you then set the sails behind the fisherman?"

And he to him: "You first sent me toward Parnassus to drink in its caves, and first you enlightened me with God.

You acted like one who goes at night, who carries the light behind him and does not benefit himself, but afterward makes people learned,

when you said: 'The century is renewed; justice and the first human era return, and a new progeny descends from heaven.'

For you I was a poet, for you a Christian: but so that you may see better what I draw, I will stretch out my hand to color.

The world was already entirely pregnant with true belief, sown by the messages of the eternal kingdom;

and your words, touched upon above, were in tune with the new preachers; so I became accustomed to visiting them.

Then they came to seem so holy to me that, when Domitian persecuted them, their laments were not heard without my tears;

and while they remained there with me, I remembered them, and their upright customs made me despise all other sects.

And before I led the Greeks to the rivers of Thebes in my poetry, I was baptized; but out of fear I remained a Christian,

long displaying paganism; and this tepidity made me circle the fourth circle more than the fourth hundredth.

You, then, who have lifted the lid that hid from me all the good I say, while we have too much to climb,

tell me where our ancient Terrentius is, Caecilius, Plautus, and Varro, if you know: tell me if they are damned, and in which alley."

"These and Persius and I and many others," my leader replied, "are with that Greek who nurses the Muses more than anyone else,

in the first circle of the blind prison. We often talk of the mountain that always has our nurses with it.

Euripides is ours, and Antiphon,
Simonides, Agathon, and others more
and Greeks who once adorn their brows with laurel.

There are seen of your people Antigone, Deiphile, and Argia, and Ismene as sad as she was.

She is seen whom Langia pointed out; there is the daughter of Tiresias, and Thetis, and with her sisters Deidamia.

Both poets were already silent, once again intent on gazing around, free from stairs and walls;

and already the four maids had fallen behind the day, and the fifth was at the pole, still raising its burning horn,

when my leader: "I believe that at the limit we must turn our right shoulders, circling the mountain as we are accustomed to do."

So custom became our guide there, and we set out with less suspicion because of the assent of that worthy soul.

They went in front, and I alone behind, and I listened to their speeches, which gave me the understanding to write poetry.

But soon our sweet conversation was interrupted by a tree we found halfway, with apples sweet and fragrant;

and as a fir tree on high slopes from branch to branch, so it slopes downward, I believe, so that no one can ascend.

From the side where our path was blocked, a clear liquid fell from the high rock and spread through the leaves above.

The two poets approached the tree, and a voice from among the branches cried out: "You will cherish this food."

Then it said: "Mary thought more about how the wedding would be honorable and complete, than about her mouth, which now responds for you.

And the ancient Roman women, drinking their water, were content with water; and Daniel scorned food and acquired wisdom.

The first century, as beautiful as gold, made acorns savory with hunger, and every stream nectar with thirst.

Apples and locusts were the food that fed the Baptist in the desert; for he is glorious and as great

as the Gospel reveals to you."

Purgatorio · Canto XXIII

While I was fixing my eyes on the green foliage, as one is wont to do who wastes his life chasing birds,

the more than father said to me: "Daughters, come now, for the time that is appointed for us must be shared more usefully."

I turned my face, and my step no less quickly, toward the wise men, who spoke so that my going cost me nothing.

And behold, weeping and singing was heard, "My labia, Lord," in such a way that it brought forth delight and pain.

"O sweet Father, what is this I hear?" I began; and he: "Shades who are perhaps unraveling the knot of their fate."

Just as thoughtful pilgrims do, coming upon unknown people on the road, who turn toward them and do not stop,

so behind us, more quickly moving, coming and passing by, a silent and devout crowd of souls admired us.

In each eye was dark and hollow, in the face pale, and so emaciated that the skin took on the color of the bones.

I do not believe that Erysichthon was so withered by fasting, when he most feared it.

I said to myself, thinking: "Behold the people who lost Jerusalem when Mary gave birth to her son!"

The eye sockets seemed like rings without gems: whoever reads "man" in the faces of men would have recognized the "M" there.

Who would believe that the smell of an apple could so govern itself, generating longing, as that of water, without knowing how?

I was already wondering what so hungers them, for the still undisclosed cause of their emaciation and their sad scales,

and behold, from the depths of his head, a shadow turned its eyes toward me and gazed intently; then it cried out loudly: "What grace is this for me?"

I would never have recognized his face; but in his voice it was clear to me what his appearance had captured.

This spark rekindled my consciousness at the changed lips, and I recognized Forese's face.

"Ah, do not contend with the dry scab that discolors my skin," he prayed, "nor with the defect of flesh I have;

but tell me the truth about yourself, say who those two souls are who are escorting you there; do not hesitate to speak to me!"

"Your face, which I once wept for when you were dead, now gives me no less pain to weep," I replied, "seeing it so distorted.

So tell me, for God's sake, what leaves you so bare; do not make me speak while I marvel, for he who is filled with another desire can speak ill."

And he to me: "From the eternal counsel falls virtue in the water and in the plant left behind from which I am so thinned.

All these people who sing while crying to indulge their gluttony beyond measure, here become saints again in hunger and thirst.

The scent that comes from the apple and the spray that spreads over its foliage kindles our longing for food and drink.

And not just once, as this space circulates, does it refresh our pain: I say pain, and I should say pleasure,

for that same desire leads us to the trees that led Christ joyfully to say 'Heli,' when he freed us with his veins."

And I to him: "Forese, from that day on which you changed the world to a better life, five years have not passed until now.

If before your power to sin was exhausted, the hour of good sorrow that remarries us to God came,

how did you come up here again? I thought I would find you down there, where time after time is refreshed."

Whereupon he said to me: "So soon has my Nella, with her bitter weeping, led me to drink the sweet absinthe of torment.

With her devout prayers and sighs, she has drawn me from the hillside where she awaits me, and freed me from the other circles.

My little widow, whom I loved dearly, is dearer and more beloved to God, the more she is alone in doing good;

for the Barbagia of Sardinia is far more modest in its women than the Barbagia where I left her.

O sweet friar, what do you want me to say? A future time is already before me, to which this hour will not be very ancient,

in which it will be forbidden for the shameless Florentine women to go about in the pulpit displaying their breasts.
