What barbarians were there, what Saracens, who needed spiritual or other disciplines to keep them hidden?

But if the shameless ones were certain of what the swift heaven prepares for them, their mouths would already be open to scream;

for, if my foresight here does not deceive me, I will be sad before he who now consoles himself with sleep covers his cheeks with fur.

Ah, brother, now make sure you no longer hide me! See that not only I, but all these people gaze there where the sun veils them."

So I to him: "If you recall what you were with me, and what I was with you, the present remembrance will still be heavy.

From that life this one who goes before me turned me back the day before yesterday, when his sister appeared round to you."

And I pointed to the sun; "He has led me through the deep night of the truly dead, with this true flesh that follows him.

Then his comforts have drawn me up, climbing and circling the mountain that straightens you who have made the world crooked.

He so insists on making me his companion that I will be where Beatrice is; there it is fitting that I remain without him.

It is Virgil who tells me this," and he pointed to him; "and this other is that shade through which just now every slope shook

your kingdom, which clears it from itself."

Purgatorio · Canto XXIV

Neither speaking nor he slowed the going any slower, but talking we went fast, like a ship blown by a good wind;

and the shades, which seemed dead things, through the hollows of my eyes drew admiration of me, aware of my life.

And I, continuing my discourse, said: "She goes up perhaps more slowly than she would, for someone else's sake.

But tell me, if you know, where Piccarda is; tell me if I see anyone of note among these people who look at me so."

"My sister, who between beautiful and good I know not which was more, triumphs joyfully on high Olympus already with her crown."

So he said first; and then: "Here it is not forbidden to name each one, since our likeness is so drained away by diet.

This," and he pointed with his finger, "is Bonagiunta, Bonagiunta da Lucca; and that face beyond him was more embossed than the others

He had the Holy Church in his arms: he was from Torso, and by fasting he purges the eels of Bolsena and the Vernaccia."

He named many others to me one by one; and they all seemed content with the naming, so much so that I saw not a single grim expression.

I saw Ubaldin da la Pila, hungry for food, and Bonifazio, who fed many people with his spear.

I saw Messer Marchese, who had already had time to drink at Forlì with less dryness, and was such that he did not feel satiated.

But as one does who looks and then values ​​one more than another, I did to the man from Lucca, who seemed to have the most knowledge of me.

He murmured; and I know not what "Gentucca" I heard there, where he felt the wound of justice that plucks him so.

"O soul," I said, "who seems so eager to speak with me, make me understand you, and satisfy you and me with your speech."

"She was born a woman, and she does not yet wear the bandage," he began, "that my city will please you, however much one reproaches it.

You will go away with this foresight: if in my murmuring you were mistaken, I will also declare the truth to you.

But say if I see here the one who once drew the new rhymes, beginning 'Ladies who have understanding of love.'"

And I to him: "I am one who, when Love inspires me, I note, and in the way that is said within me I mean."

"O brother, now I see," he said, "the knot that the Notary and Guittone and me held back from the sweet new style that I hear!

I see clearly how your pens are tightening behind the dictator, which certainly has not happened to ours;

and he who sets his heart on pleasing no longer sees the line between one style and the other"; and, almost contented, he fell silent.

As the birds that winter along the Nile sometimes form a flock in the air, then fly more quickly and march in a line,

so all the people there, turning their faces, quickened their pace, both because of their leanness and because of their light desire.

And as a man who is tired of trotting lets his companions go and strolls on until the crowding in the chest is relieved,

he let the holy flock of Forese pass by, and followed me, saying: "When will I see you again?"

"I do not know," I replied, "how long I will live; but my return will not be so fast that I will first reach the shore with my will;

because the place where I was placed to live is being depleted day by day of more good, and seems destined for sad ruin."

"Now go," he said; "for I see the one most to blame being dragged at the tail of a beast toward the valley where he never exonerates himself.

The beast goes faster with each step, growing ever larger, until it strikes him and leaves his body vilely destroyed.

Those wheels do not have long to turn," and he raised his eyes to heaven, "for it to be clear to you what my words can no longer explain.

You stay now; for time is precious in this kingdom, so that I waste too much by coming with you so evenly."

As a cavalier sometimes gallops out of his horse's ranks and goes to gain honor at the first obstacle,

so he departed from us with greater passes; and I remained on the road with him, the two who were beyond the world such great marshals.

And when he entered before us, and my eyes became followers of him, as my mind was of his words,

the heavy and lively branches of another apple tree appeared to me, and not far away, for I had just then turned to the rocks.

I saw people beneath it raise their hands and cry out I know not what toward the leaves, like longing and vain children

who pray, and the one prayed to does not respond, but, to make the desire very keen, holds their desire high and does not hide it.

Then he departed, as if changed; and we now came to the great tree, which rejects so many prayers and tears.

"Pass on without coming nearer: there is a tree higher up that was bitten by Eve, and this plant arose from it."

So among the branches I do not know who was saying; whereupon Virgil, Statius, and I, crowded together, went on toward the upward direction.

"Remember," he said, "the cursed ones formed in the clouds, whom, sated, Theseus fought with his double breasts;

and the Hebrews who showed themselves weak at drinking, because Gideon did not want them as companions when he descended the hills toward Midian."

So, approaching one of the two edges, we passed, hearing the sins of gluttony followed already by miserable gains.

Then, widening along the single road, a full thousand paces and more carried us on, each gazing without a word.

"What are you three thinking about?" a sudden voice called out, and I started, as frightened and cowardly beasts do.

I raised my head to see who I was; and never before had glass or metals been seen in a furnace so bright and red,

when I saw one who said: "If you wish to climb, you must turn here; here goes he who would go in peace."

His appearance had taken my sight away, so I turned behind my teachers, like one who goes according to his hearing.

And as, heralding the dawn, the May breeze moves and smells sweet, all impregnated with grass and flowers;

I felt a wind blow across my forehead, and I clearly felt the feathers move, making the scent of ambrosia linger.

And I heard them say: "Blessed are those who are so brightly illuminated by grace that the love of pleasure does not smolder in their breasts with excessive desire,

always exhausting what is just!"

Purgatorio · Canto XXV

Now was the time when I did not want to cripple the ascent; for the sun had left the circle of noon to Taurus and night to Scorpio.

Therefore, as a man does who does not rest but goes his own way, whatever appears to him, if need be pierced by a stimulus,

so we entered the alley, one before the other taking the ladder that by its skill displaces climbers.

And like the little stork that raises its wing for the desire to fly, and dares not abandon the nest, but lowers it;

such was I, with a burning and quenching desire to ask, reaching the point of one who attempts to speak.

He did not let go of my sweet father, though he was swift in his pace, but said: "Shoot the bow of speech, which you have drawn to the point."

Then I opened my mouth confidently and began: "How can one grow lean where the need to feed does not touch?"

"If you remembered how Meleager was consumed by the burning of a firebrand, this would not be so bitter to you," he said;

and if you thought how, at your flicker, your image flickers within the mirror, what seems hard would seem withered to you.

But since you rest within your will, behold, Statius is here; and I call upon him and pray that he may now be the healer of your wounds."

"If I free him from the eternal vision," Statius replied, "where you are, you excuse me for not being able to deny you."

Then he began: "If your mind, son, looks at and receives my words, I will enlighten you as to how you spoke.

Perfect blood, which is not drunk from thirsty veins, and remains as food taken from the table,

takes into the heart of all human members the informative virtue, like that which makes them through vain veins.

Still digested, it descends where it is better to remain silent than to speak; and from there it moans upon the blood of others in a natural vessel.

There both are gathered together, one ready to suffer, the other to act, because of the perfect place where it presses;

and, once it has arrived, it begins to work, first coagulating, and then animating that which it has caused to exist through its matter.

Soul, having become the active virtue like that of a plant, so different that one is on its way and that one has already reached the shore,

then works so much that it already moves and feels, like a seaweed; and then it begins to organize the powers from which it is seed.

Now, my son, the virtue that is from the heart of the generator, where nature intends all its members, unfolds itself.

But how from an animal he becomes a child, you do not yet see: this is the point that has already made one wiser than you err,

so that by his doctrine he separated the possible intellect from the soul, because he saw no organ assumed by it.

Open your heart to the truth that comes; and know that, as soon as the articulation of the brain is perfect in the fetus,

the prime mover turns to it, joyfully addressing so much of nature's art, and breathes forth a new spirit, replete with virtue,

which draws into its substance what it finds active there, and becomes one soul, living and feeling and revolving within itself.

And so that you may admire the word less, consider the heat of the sun becoming wine, combined with the juice that flows from the vine.

When Lachesis no longer has any flax, she dissolves from the flesh, and by virtue carries away both the human and the divine:

all the other powers are mute; memory, intelligence, and will are in action much more acute than before.

Without stopping, she falls miraculously to one of the shores; there she first knows her paths.

As soon as a place encircles it there, the formative power radiates around it, as much as it does in the living limbs.

And just as the air, when it is very pure, becomes adorned with various colors by the rays of another reflected in it;

so the nearby air settles there and in the form that is within it virtually seals the soul that remained there;

and then, like the flame that follows the fire wherever it changes, the spirit follows its new form.

Because it then has its appearance, it is called a shadow; and from this it then organizes each feeling, up to the point of sight.

From this we speak, and from this we repeat; from this we shed the tears and sighs that you may have heard on the mountain.

According to how desires and other emotions afflict us, the shadow shapes itself; and this is the reason why you are looking."

And now we had come to the final torture, and turned to the right, and we were intent on another care.

There the bank shoots flame outward, and the cornice breathes upward that reflects it and seizes it away;

so we had to go through the open side one by one; and I feared the fire on this side, and from that I feared falling down.

My leader said: "In this place one must keep the reins tight on the eyes, because one might easily err."

Then I heard 'Summae Deus clementiae' sung in the bosom of the great ardor, which made me no less reluctant to turn;

and I saw spirits going through the flame; whereupon I looked at them and at my steps, sharing my sight from time to time.

As soon as the hymn ended, they shouted loudly: "I do not know the virus!" Then they began the hymn again, in a low voice.

When it was over, they also shouted: "Diana took refuge in the woods, and Helice drove her away, for she had smelled the poison of Venus."

Then they returned to singing; then women shouted and husbands who were chaste, as virtue and marriage dictate.

And this method, I believe, suffices for them for as long as the fire scorches them: with such care and with such meals it is necessary

that the wound heals itself.

Purgatorio · Canto XXVI

As we thus walked along the edge, one before the other, and often the good Master said to me: "Look: it is good that I trick you";

The sun struck me on my right shoulder, and already, shining, all the west was changing its aspect from white to blue;

and with my shadow I made the flame seem hotter; and yet to this sign I saw many shadows, as I went, pay attention.

This was the reason that made them begin to speak of me; and they began to say: "He does not seem a fictitious body."

Then, as far as they could, some turned toward me, always careful not to go where they would not be burned.

"O you who go, not to be slower, but perhaps reverent, after the others, answer me who burn with thirst and fire.

Nor is your answer needed by me alone; for all these thirst for it more than for cold water an Indian or Ethiopian.

Tell us how you make yourself a wall to the sun, even as if you had not yet entered the net of death."

One of them spoke to me in this way; and I would already have revealed myself, had I not been attentive to another novelty that then appeared;

for in the middle of the burning path people came facing this one, which made me gaze in suspense.

There I see every shade hurrying from every side, kissing one another without stopping, content with a brief celebration;

so within them, a dark troop of ants snuffles one another, perhaps to spy on their path and their fortune.

As soon as they depart from the friendly welcome, before the first step has passed, each one labors to shout again:

the new folk: "Sodom and Gomorrah!" and the other: "Pasiphae enters the cow, so that the bull may run to his lust."

Then, like cranes that fly toward the Riphae mountains, some of them shun the frost, some of them the sun,

one folk goes away, another comes; and they return, weeping, to their first songs and to the shouts that are most fitting for them;

And they approached me, as if before me, the very ones who had begged me, attentive to listen in their faces.

I, who had twice seen them grateful, began: "O souls, certain of having, whenever it may be, a state of peace,

my limbs have not remained immature or mature over there, but are here with me with its blood and its joints.

Up here I go, so as not to be blind any more; a lady is above who gains grace for me, for which I bring the mortal through your world.

But if your greater desire is soon satiated, so that heaven, which is full of love and extends more widely, may shelter you,

tell me, so that I may write more pages of it, who you are, and who is that crowd that goes behind your backs."

The mountaineer is no more stunned and silent in his gaze, when he enters the city, rough and wild,

than each shade did in its own way; but when they were freed from amazement, which soon subsides in the lofty hearts,

"Blessed are you, who from our marches," she who first asked me began again, "to embark on experience, to die better!

The people who do not come with us are offended by that for which Caesar, triumphant, has already heard himself called "Queen":

Therefore they depart, crying "Sodom," reproaching themselves as you have heard, and aiding the burning heat in shame.

Our sin was hermaphrodite; But because we did not obey human law, following our appetite like beasts,

to our shame, the name of her who became bestial in the bestial splinters is read for us when we depart.

Now you know our deeds and what we were guilty of: if perhaps you want to know who we are by name, there is no time to say, and I would not know.

I will make you want to be rid of me: I am Guido Guinizzelli, and I am already purging myself to grieve well before I reach the end."

As in the sadness of Ligurgo two sons became when they saw their mother again, so I became, but I do not rise to such a degree,

when I hear my father name himself and the others, my best, who ever used sweet and graceful love rhymes;

And without hearing or speaking, I went on thoughtfully gazing at him for a long time, nor, because of the fire, did I draw any closer.

After I had fed on gazing, I offered myself fully ready to his service with the affirmations that make others believe.

And he to me: "You leave such a trace, from what I hear, in me, and so clear, that Lethe cannot take it away or make it gray.

But if your words now swore true, tell me what reason you have for showing in your words and your looks that you hold me dear."

And I to him: "Your sweet sayings, which, as long as modern usage lasts, will still make their incense dear."

"O brother," he said, "this one whom I point out to you with my finger," and he pointed to a spirit ahead, "was a better smith of maternal speech.

Love verses and romance prose surpassed all; and leave the fools to say that they believe Lemosì to be superior.

They direct their faces to the voice rather than to the truth, and thus they confirm his opinion before art or reason are heard for them.

So did many ancients of Guittone, shouting after shout, always praising him, until truth has conquered him with more people.

Now if you have such ample privilege that it is permitted for you to go to the cloister where Christ is, abbot of the college,

tell him for me a paternoster, as much as we need in this world, where the power to sin is no longer ours."

Then, perhaps to make room for others according to his proximity, he disappeared into the fire, like a fish sinking into the water.

I stepped forward a little and said that my desire was preparing a gracious place for his name.

He began to speak freely: "So much your courts will adorn me tomorrow, that I cannot or will not cover you.

I am Arnaut, who will cry and sing; I see the past, and I will see the joy I hope for tomorrow.

May your prayers, by that valor that guides you to the top of the stairs, often in times of my pain!"

Then he hid himself in the fire that refines them.

Purgatorio · Canto XXVII

As when the first rays shimmer where its Maker shed his blood, when Iberus fell beneath lofty Libra,

and the waves in the Ganges were scorched by noon, so stood the sun; where the day was departing, as the angel of God joyfully appeared to us.

Out of the flame he stood on the shore, and sang "Blessed are the world's chords!" in a voice far more powerful than ours.

Then he said, "There is no further progress, unless the fire bites first, holy souls: enter into it, and do not be deaf to the singing beyond,"

he said to us as we approached him; so that, when I heard him, I became like one who is thrown into the grave.

I stretched out my clasped hands, gazing at the fire and vividly imagining human bodies I had once seen alight.

The good escorts turned toward me; And Virgil said to me: "My son, there may be torment here, but not death.

Remember, remember! And if I guided you safely to Geryon, what shall I do now, closer to God?

Believe for certain that if you were to remain within the heart of this flame for a full thousand years, it could not make you bald.

And if perhaps you think I am deceiving you, move toward her, and make her believe with your hands on the hem of your robe.

Put down now, put down all fear; turn this way and come: enter safely!" And I still stood firm, against my conscience.

When he saw me standing still and stern, a little troubled, he said: "Now see, son: between Beatrice and you is this wall."

As at the name of Thisbe, Pyramus, upon death, opened his eyes and gazed at her, when the mulberry tree turned red;

so, my hardness softened, I turned to my wise guide, hearing the name that always wells up in my mind.

He shook his brow and said, "What! Do you want us to stay here?" Then he smiled as one does to a child who is overcome by the apple.

Then he placed himself before me in the fire, begging Statius to come back, who had previously separated us on a long road.

As soon as I was inside, I would have thrown myself into a boiling glass to cool myself, so meterless was the fire there.

My sweet father, to comfort me, was still speaking of Beatrice, saying, "I seem to see her eyes already."

A voice singing from beyond guided us; and we, still attentive to her, came out to where the mountain was rising.

'Come, blessed Father of mine,' sounded within a light that was there, so that it overcame me that I could not look at it.

'The sun is going away,' it added, 'and evening is coming; do not stop, but study your pace, while the west does not darken.'

The path rose straight through the rock toward such a place that I blocked out the rays before me of the sun, which was already low.

And we raised the stairs a few steps, so that the sun, through the fading shadow, we felt behind us, both I and my students.

And before in all its immense parts there was a horizon made of a single aspect, and night had all its dispensations,

each of us made bed on a step; for the nature of the mountain deprived us of the power to ascend further and to enjoy it.

As goats are ruminating, gentle, having stood swift and bold on the peaks before they have eaten,

silent in the shade, while the sun blazes, watched by the shepherd, who leans on his staff and serves them in repose;

and as the herdsman who lodges outside, spends the night beside his sheepfold, quietly watching lest the wild beast scatter it;

such were we then, I like a goat, and they like shepherds, swathed here and there in the high cave.

Little could be seen there of the outside; but, for that little, I saw the stars brighter and larger than usual.

Thus ruminating and gazing at them, sleep overtook me; Sleep, which often, before the fact is done, knows the news.

At the hour, I believe, when the east first shone on Mount Cytherea, which always seems ablaze with the fire of love,

In a dream, I seemed to see a young and beautiful woman walking across a plain picking flowers; and singing, she said:

"Let whoever asks my name know that I am Lia, and I move my lovely hands around to make myself a garland.

To please myself in the mirror, I adorn myself here; but my sister Rachel never leaves her gaze, and sits all day.

She is as eager to see her beautiful eyes as I am to adorn myself with my hands; she satisfies the sight, and me the work."

And already through the pre-dawn splendors, which rise more pleasing to pilgrims the more, returning, they lodge less distant,

the darkness fled on all sides, and my sleep with it; whereupon I arose, seeing the great masters already risen.

"That sweet apple which the care of mortals searches through so many branches, today will put your hunger at peace."

Virgil spoke these same words to me; and never were there gifts that were as pleasing as these.

So great a desire came upon me to be above, that with every step I felt my feathers grow in flight.

When the stairway was completely traversed beneath us and we reached the highest step, Virgil fixed his eyes on me,

and said: "You have seen the temporal fire and the eternal, my son; and you have come to a place where I cannot discern further.

I have drawn you here with wit and art; now take your pleasure as your guide; leave the steep paths, leave art.

See the sun shining on your forehead; see the grass, the flowers, and the shrubs that the earth alone produces here.

While those beautiful eyes, which, weeping, brought me to you, are gladdened, you may sit and walk among them.

Do not wait for my words or my signs any longer; your will is free, upright, and sound, and do not do it by its own will:

Therefore I crown and mitre you upon you."

Purgatorio · Canto XXVIII

Already eager to search within and around the divine forest, thick and living, that tempered the new day in my eyes,

Without waiting any longer, I left the shore, slowly making my way across the plain, over soil that smelled on all sides.

A sweet breeze, without change, struck my forehead with no more force than a gentle wind;

whereby the trembling leaves, all of them ready, bent toward the side where the holy mountain casts its first shadow;

but not so far from their uprightness that the little birds on the summits ceased to practice all their art;

But with full joy they received the first hours, singing among the leaves, which supported their rhymes,

just as it gathers from branch to branch through the pine forest on the shore of Chiassi, when Aeolus releases it.

My slow steps had already carried me so far into the ancient forest that I could not see where I had entered;

and then, behold, a stream prevented me from going any further, which, with its little waves, bent the grass that had grown on its bank toward the left.

All the waters that are purest here seemed to have within them some mixture of that which hides nothing,

even though it moves dark brown under the perpetual shadow, which never allows the sun or the moon to shine there.

I stopped my feet and passed my eyes beyond the stream, to gaze at the great change of the fresh air;

and there appeared to me, as something suddenly appears that distracts all other thoughts with wonder,

a lonely lady who was singing and choosing flower from flower that painted her entire path.

"Ah, beautiful lady, who warms herself with the rays of love, if I want to believe the faces that usually bear witness to the heart,

become inclined to draw forward," I said to her, "toward this river, so that I can hear you singing.

You make me remember where and what Proserpina was at the time she lost her mother, she, and she, spring."

As a dancing lady turns, her feet pressed to the ground and within herself, and barely sets foot forward,

she turned toward me over the vermilion and yellow flowers, no different from a virgin whose pure eyes she casts;

and she granted my prayers, drawing so close that the sweet sound reached me with its meaning.

As soon as she reached the spot where the grass is already bathed by the waves of the beautiful river, she granted me the gift of raising her eyes.

I do not believe that such light shone under the brows of Venus, pierced by her son beyond her usual wont.

She smiled from the other bank, straight ahead, drawing more color with her hands than the high, seedless earth casts.

The river took us three paces away; But Hellespont, where Xerxes passed, still a curb on all human pride,

did not suffer more hatred from Leander for surging between Sestos and Abydos than it did from me for not opening then.

"You are new, and perhaps because I laugh," she began, "in this place chosen for human nature as its nest,

marveling, you harbor some suspicion; but the psalm Delectasti sheds light, which can clear your intellect.

And you who are before me and prayed to me, say if you wish to hear more; for I have come ready to answer all your questions, sufficiently."

"The water," I said, "and the sound of the forest impugn within me a new belief in something I heard contrary to this."

So she said: "I will tell you how that which makes you marvel comes from its cause, and I will clear away the fog that afflicts you.

The Supreme Good, who alone pleases Himself, made man good and for good, and gave him this place as a pledge of eternal peace.

Through his fault he stayed here for a short time; through his fault he exchanged honest laughter and sweet play for weeping and anguish.

So that the disturbance caused by the exhalation of water and earth, which pursue the heat as much as they can,

would not wage war against man, this mountain rose so high toward the sky, and is free from the depths where it is enclosed.

Now because all the air revolves in a circle with the first turn, if its circle is not broken at some point,

in this height which is entirely dissolved in the living air, such a motion strikes and makes the forest resound because it is dense;

and the struck plant has such power that it impregnates the air with its virtue and then, circling, shakes it around;

and the other earth, according to its own and its heavenly worthyness, conceives and gives birth to diverse wood of diverse virtues.

It would not seem surprising there, hearing this, when a plant takes root there without visible seed.

And you must know that the holy field where you are is full of every seed, and contains within itself fruit that cannot be crushed there.

The water you see does not rise from a vein that restores vapor that converts ice, like a river that gains and loses breath;

but it issues from a firm and certain fountain, which recovers as much from the will of God as it pours out from two sides.

From this side it descends with virtue that removes from others the memory of sin; from the other it restores it with every good deed.

From here Lethe; Thus on the other side is Eunoe called, and it is of no use unless first tasted here and there:

This is above all other flavors. And although your thirst may be sufficiently sated for me to reveal no more to you,

I will give you yet another corollary, by grace; nor do I believe my words are less dear to you if they extend beyond my promise to you.

Those who in ancient times wrote poetry about the golden age and its happy state, perhaps dreamed of this place on Parnassus.

Here was the human root innocent; here springtime always and every fruit; this is nectar of which everyone speaks.

I then turned back completely to my poets, and saw that they had heard the last construction with a smile;

then my face returned to the beautiful lady.

Purgatorio · Canto XXIX

Singing like a lady in love, she continued with the end of her words: "Blessed are those who have sinned!"

And like nymphs wandering alone through the wild shades, some longing to see, some to flee the sun,

then she moved against the river, going up the bank; and I, like her, followed little by little.

There were not a hundred steps between hers and mine, when I turned the banks equally, so that I returned to the east.

Nor had we gone far when the lady turned to me, saying, "My brother, look and listen."

And behold, a sudden brightness passed everywhere through the great forest, such that I was afraid of lightning.

But because the lightning, as it comes, stops, and the other, as it lasted, shone brighter and brighter, I said in my thoughts, "What is this?"

And a sweet melody ran through the luminous air; whereupon good zeal made me recover the courage of Eve,

who, where earth and heaven obeyed, female, alone and newly formed, could not endure to remain under any veil;

under which, had she been devout, I would have experienced those ineffable delights I had experienced before and for a longer time.

While I wandered among so many first fruits of eternal pleasure, all suspenseful and yearning for even greater joys,

before us, like a blazing fire, the air beneath the green branches grew; and the sweet sound was already heard in song.

O most holy Virgins, if I have ever endured hunger, cold, or vigils for your sake, it is reason that I call upon you for mercy.

Now Helicon must verse for me, and Urania must help me with her chorus to put powerful thoughts into verse.

A little further on, seven golden trees distorted the appearance of the long stretch of the middle that still lay between us and them;

But when I was so close to them that the common object, which deceives the senses, did not lose any of its effect through distance,

the virtue that prepares reasoned discourse, learned how they were candelabra, and in the voices of the singing of "Hosanna."

Above, the beautiful instrument blazed far brighter than the moon in the clear sky at midnight in its mid-month.

I turned in full admiration to good Virgil, and he answered me with a look no less filled with wonder.

Then I returned my gaze to the lofty things that were moving toward us so slowly that they would be overcome by new brides.

The lady rebuked me: "Why do you only burn so with the desire for the living lights, and do not look at what comes behind them?"

Then I saw people, as if following their leaders, coming after them, dressed in white; and such whiteness had never been seen here.

The water was pouring in from the left flank, and, if I looked into it, it reflected my left side like a mirror.

When I had such a view from my bank that only the river made me distant, I paused to see better,

and I saw the flames go ahead, leaving behind them the painted air, and they had the appearance of drawn brushes;

so that above there remained seven distinct bands, all in those colors with which the Sun makes its bow and Delia her girdle.

These banners, behind me, were greater than my sight; and, as far as I could tell, those outside were ten paces distant.

Beneath a sky so beautiful as I was divided, twenty-four elders, two by two, came crowned with fleur-de-lis.

They all sang: "Blessed are you among the daughters of Adam, and blessed be your beauties forever!"

After the flowers and other fresh grasses opposite me on the other bank were freed from those chosen people,

as light follows light in heaven, four animals came after them, each crowned with green leaves.

Each was feathered with six wings; the feathers were full of eyes; and the eyes of Argus, if they were alive, would be like this.

I waste no more rhymes to describe their forms, reader; for another expense constrains me, so that I cannot be generous with this;

But read Ezekiel, who paints them as he saw them coming from the cold region with wind and cloud and fire;

and such as you will find in his maps, such were they here, except that John is with me on the wings and separates from him.

The space between them four contained a chariot, on two wheels, triumphant, drawn on the neck of a griffin.

It stretched both wings upward between the middle one and the three and three stripes, so that, cleaving, it hurt nothing.

They rose so high that they were not seen; the limbs were golden as a bird, and the others white, mingled with vermilion.

Not that Rome delighted Africanus, or indeed Augustus, with such a beautiful chariot, but the Sun's would have been poor with it;

that of the Sun who, straying, was burned by the prayer of the devout Earth, when Jupiter was mysteriously just.

Three women came dancing in a circle from the right wheel; one so red she was barely recognizable in the fire;

the other was as if her flesh and bones had been made of emerald; the third seemed like newly stirred snow;

and now they seemed drawn by the white one, now by the red one; and from the side of this one the others took their steps, slow and swift.

On the left, four were making merry, dressed in purple, following in the footsteps of one of them who had three eyes in her head.

After all the aforementioned knot I saw two old men in odd clothing, but similar in demeanor, modest and solid.

One showed himself to be one of the servants of that great Hippocrates, whom nature has made the animals she holds dearest;

the other showed the opposite concern with a sharp and shining sword, such that it struck fear into me from this side of the river.

Then I saw four in humble attire, and behind them all, a single old man, sleeping, with a witty face.

And these seven were accustomed to the first band, but around their heads they did not make a grove of lilies,

rather of roses and other vermilion flowers; I would have sworn that not far off I expected them all to be burning from above their brows.

And when the chariot was opposite me, a thunder was heard, and those worthy folk seemed to have their progress more hampered,

stopping there with the first standards.

Purgatorio · Canto XXX

When the north of the first heaven, which never knew setting, nor garden, nor any mist but a veil of sin,

and which made each one there aware of his duty, as the lowest does when a ship turns to reach port,

stood still: the true folk, having come first between the griffin and it, turned to the chariot as to their peace;

and one of them, as if sent from heaven, cried out three times, singing, "Veni, sponsa, de Libano!" and all the others followed suit.

As the blessed at the newest announcement will quickly rise, each from his cave, halting with their robed voices,

so on the divine throne rose a hundred, with so many voices, ministers and messengers of eternal life.

All said, "Benedictus qui venis!" and, throwing flowers above and around, "Manibus, oh, give lily fullness!"

I already saw, at the beginning of the day, the eastern sky all rosy, and the other sky adorned with beautiful serenity;

and the face of the rising sun was shadowed, so that through the temperate vapors the eye could sustain it for a long time:

Thus within a cloud of flowers that rose from angelic hands and fell back in and out,

a woman appeared to me, girded with olive-trees above a white veil, dressed in the color of living flame beneath a green mantle.

And my spirit, which had been so long without being amazed at her presence, trembling, broken,

without having any further knowledge of the eyes, by the occult power that came from her, felt the great power of ancient love.

As soon as the high virtue that had already pierced me before I left childhood struck my sight,

I turned to my left with the reluctance with which a child runs to his mother when he is afraid or when he is afflicted,

to say to Virgil: "Less than a drachma of blood remains to me that does not tremble: I know the signs of the ancient flame."

But Virgil had left us bereft of himself, Virgil, sweetest father, Virgil to whom I gave me for my salvation;

nor although I lost my ancient mother, did it avail my cheeks, cleansed of dew, to keep them from turning dark, weeping.

"Dante, because Virgil is leaving, do not weep yet, do not weep yet; for weeping is fitting for another sword."

Like an admiral who comes prow and stern to see the people who minister on the other vessels, and encourages them to do well;

on the left bank of the chariot, when I turned at the sound of my name, which must be recorded here,

I saw the lady who before appeared to me veiled under the angelic feast, direct her eyes toward me from this side of the river.

Although the veil that descended from her head, encircled by the leaves of Minerva, did not allow her to appear manifest,

regal in her act, still haughty, she continued, like one who speaks and reserves the most passionate speech for himself:

"Look at us well! I am truly, truly I am Beatrice. How did you deigned to approach the mountain? Did you not know that here is the happy man?"

My eyes fell into the clear fountain; But seeing myself in it, I dragged myself to the grass, so much shame weighed on my brow.

So the mother seems proud to her son, as she seemed to me; for she tastes bitterly the bitter pity.

She was silent; and the angels suddenly sang, "In te, Domine, speravi"; but they did not pass beyond "peses meos."

Just as snow freezes between the living beams along the back of Italy, blown and pressed by the enslaved winds,

then, melted, seeps into itself, provided the earth that loses its shadow breathes, so that it seems like fire melting the candle;

so I was without tears and sighs before the singing of those who always note the notes of the eternal circles;

But after I heard their sweet tempers pitying me, it seemed as if they had said: 'Lady, why do you melt it so?'

The ice that had constricted my heart changed into spirit and water, and with anguish it flowed from my mouth and eyes.

She, still standing firm on the aforementioned side of the chariot, then addressed the pious souls thus:

"You watch in the eternal day, so that night and sleep may not steal from you any step that the world takes along its paths.

Therefore, my answer is with greater care that he who weeps there understand me, because it is guilt and grief of one measure.

Not only by the work of the mighty wheels, which direct each seed to some goal according to the companionship of the stars,

but by the generosity of divine graces, which have such lofty vapors to rain them down that our sights do not approach there,

this man was such in his new life, so virtually, that every skillful habit would have found marvelous proof in him.

But the more malignant and wild the earth becomes with bad and uncultivated seed, the more it has of good earthly vigor.

For a while I supported him with my face: showing him my youthful eyes, I led him with me to the right path.

As soon as I reached the threshold of my second age and changed my life, he took himself from me and gave it to someone else.

When I had risen from flesh to spirit, and beauty and virtue had grown in me, I was less dear and less welcome to him;

and he turned his steps along an untrue path, following false images of good, which make no promise complete.

Nor did imploring inspirations avail me, with which, in dreams and otherwise, I called him back: so little did he care for him!

He fell so low that all the means for his salvation were already exhausted, except showing him the lost people.

For this reason I visited the gate of the dead, and to him who brought him up here, my prayers, weeping, were brought.

A high fate of God would be broken, if Lethe were to pass and such a dish were tasted without any penalty

of repentance that sheds tears."

Purgatorio · Canto XXXI

"O you who are beyond the sacred river," turning his speech to me with a point, which had seemed harsh to me even at the edge,

he began again, continuing without hesitation, "say, say if this is true: to such a charge your confession must be joined."

My strength was so confused that my voice moved and died before it was released from its organs.

He suffered but a little; then he said: "What are you thinking? Answer me; for the sad memories in you are not yet wounded by the water."

Confusion and fear mixed together forced such a "yes" from my mouth that my eyes needed to understand.

As a crossbow breaks, when it releases its string and bow from too much tension, and with less force the shaft hits the mark,

so I burst under my heavy burden, tears and sighs gushing out, and my voice slackened in its passage.

Whereupon she said to me: "Through my desires, which led you to love the good beyond which there is nothing to aspire to,

what chasms did you cross or what chains did you find, so that you had to strip yourself of the hope of passing forward?

And what conveniences or what remains were shown in the faces of others, so that you had to walk before them?"

After the exhalation of a bitter sigh, I barely had the voice to answer, and my lips could hardly form it.

Weeping, I said: "The present things with their false pleasure turned my steps, as soon as your face was hidden."

And she: "If I were silent or if I denied what you confess, your guilt would be no less known: by such a judge you shall be stoned!

But when the accusation of sin bursts from one's own cheek, in our court the wheel turns against the edge.

Nevertheless, because now you bear shame for your error, and because another time, hearing the serene sounds, you are stronger,

put down the seed of weeping and listen: so you will hear how my buried flesh should have moved in the opposite direction.

Never did nature or art present to you such pleasure as the beautiful limbs in which I was enclosed, and which are scattered on the earth;

And if the supreme pleasure was so deprived of you by my death, what mortal thing should have drawn you to its desire?

You should have risen up behind me, who was no longer such, at the first arrow of deceitful things.

You should not have weighed down your feathers, expecting another blow, or a little girl, or some other novelty with so little use.

A new little bird waits two or three; but before the eyes of the feathered ones, a net is spread in vain or an arrow is shot.

Like children, ashamed, silent, with eyes to the ground, listening and recognizing themselves and repenting,

such was I; and she said: "When you are grieved by hearing, raise your beard, and you will grieve more by looking."

With less resistance a sturdy oak is stripped away, whether by our wind or by that of the land of Iarbas,

that I did not raise my chin at its command; and when through my beard my face asked, I well knew the venom of the argument.

And as my face relaxed, my eye understood those first creatures resting on their aspersion;

and my eyes, still unsure, saw Beatrice turned toward the beast that is one person in two natures.

Under her veil and beyond the river, she seemed to me to conquer her ancient self more, conquer the others here, when she was here.

The nettle stung me so much with repentance that of all other things, whatever most twisted me in its love, became my greatest enemy.

So much gratitude stung my heart that I fell defeated; and as I then did, she who gave me the reason rose.

Then, when my heart gave me strength again, I saw the lady I had found alone above me, and she was saying: "Hold me, hold me!"

She had drawn me into the river up to my throat, and, drawing me along, she was drifting through the water as light as a stream.

When I was near the blessed shore, I heard "Sprinkle me!" so sweetly that I cannot remember it, let alone write it.

The beautiful lady opened her arms; she clasped my head and submerged me where I must swallow the water.

Then she took me up and, bathed, offered me to the dance of the four beauties; and each of them covered me with her arm.

"Here we are nymphs and in the sky we are stars; Before Beatrice descended into the world, we were appointed her handmaidens.

You will gaze upon her eyes; but in the joyful light within, the three there, who gaze more deeply, will sharpen yours."

So they began singing; and then they led me with them to the Griffin's chest, where Beatrice stood turned toward us.

They said: "See that you do not spare your sight; we have placed you before the emeralds from which Love once drew his weapons from you."

A thousand desires, hotter than flame, tightened my eyes around the shining eyes that still rested steadfastly upon the Griffin.

As the sun shines in a mirror, so did the two-headed beast within it, now with different powers, now with different directions.

Think, reader, if I marveled when I saw the thing itself remain quiet, and transformed into its idol.

While my soul, filled with wonder and joy, savored that food which, satiating itself, thirsts for itself,

showing itself in its gestures to be of a higher tribe, the other three came forward, dancing to their angelic companion.

"Turn, Beatrice, turn your holy eyes," was her song, "to your faithful one who, to see you, has taken so many steps!

Please grant us the grace to reveal your mouth to him, so that he may discern the second beauty you conceal.

O splendor of living, eternal light, who grew so pale beneath the shadow of Parnassus, or drank from its cistern,

that his mind did not seem to be cluttered, trying to render you as you appeared there where the harmonizing heaven overshadowed you,

when you were released into the open air?

Purgatorio · Canto XXXII

So fixed and intent were my eyes, quenching their ten-year thirst, that all my other senses were extinguished.

And they, on both sides, seemed not to care—so the holy smile drew them to itself with the ancient net!—;

when those goddesses forced my face to my left, because I heard them say, "Too fixed!"

and the disposition to see that had just struck my eyes by the sun made me somewhat blind.

But after my face had returned to a little (and I say "a little" in respect to the very sensitive one from which I was forcibly removed),

I saw the glorious army turned on its right arm, and returning with the sun and the seven flames before its face.

As a company turns under shields to save itself, and turns with the signal, before it can completely transform itself;

that army of the celestial kingdom that was advancing passed entirely through us before the first barge of the chariot could bend.

Then the ladies returned to the wheels, and the Griffin moved his blessed burden so that not a feather swayed.

The beautiful lady who drew me to the pass, and Statius and I, followed the wheel that made its orbit with a smaller arc.

So, walking through the high, empty forest, the fault of her who grew into the serpent, an angelic note tempered our steps.

Perhaps in three flights the unrestrained arrow took up as much space as we had risen when Beatrice descended.

I heard everyone murmur "Adam"; then we circled a tree stripped of leaves and other foliage on each branch.

Its horn, which expands the higher it is, was admired by the people of the Indies in their woods for its height.

"Blessed are you, griffin, who do not tear with your beak this sweet-tasting wood, since it is so hard for the belly to twist."

Thus around the sturdy tree the others shouted; and the twin animal: "Thus is preserved the seed of every just thing."

And turning to the pole he had drawn, he drew it to the foot of the widow's branch, and left her branch tied to it.

Just as our plants, when the great light falls down, mixed with that which radiates from behind the celestial lark,

become swollen, and then each one renews itself with its own color, before the sun reaches his horses under another star;

opening less than rose-colored and more than violet-colored, the plant that once had such sunny branches was renewed.

I didn't understand it, nor is the hymn that those people sang here, nor did I tolerate the whole note.

If I could portray how my merciless eyes fell asleep upon hearing Syrinx, eyes for whom keeping awake cost so dearly;

like a painter painting with an example, I would depict how I fell asleep; but who wants to depict sleep well?

So I skip ahead to when I awoke, and I say that a splendor tore through the veil of sleep, and a cry of "Arise! What are you doing?"

As at the sight of the blossoms of the apple tree that makes the angels greedy for its fruit and makes a perpetual wedding in heaven,

Peter and John and James, led and conquered, returned to the word by which most of their dreams were broken,

and they saw their school diminished, both of Moses and of Elijah, and their master's robe changed;

such I returned, and I saw that pious one standing above me who had guided my steps along the river before.

And all in doubt I said: "Where is Beatrice?" To which she: "See her under the new leaf sitting at its root.

See the company that surrounds her: the others, after the griffin, go upward with sweeter and more profound song."

And whether her speech was more diffused, I know not, for she was already in my eyes who had closed me to other understanding.

She sat alone on the true earth, like a guard left there of the shield I saw tied to the biformed beast.

The seven nymphs enclosed her in a circle, holding in their hands those lights that are safe from the North and the South.

"Here you will be a sylvan for a short time; and you will be with me forever a citizen of that Rome where Christ is Roman.

Therefore, for the sake of the world that lives ill, keep your eyes on the chariot now, and write down what you see, when you return from there."

Thus Beatrice; and I, who was wholly devoted to her commandments, directed my mind and eyes where she wished.

Never did fire descend with such swift motion from a thick cloud, when it rains from that farthest boundary,

as I saw Jove's bird descend through the tree, breaking not only the bark, but also the flowers and the new leaves;

and struck the chariot with all its force, whereupon it tilted like a ship in a storm, overcome by the waves, now to luff, now to luff.

Then I saw a fox, seemingly starved of all good food, leap into the cradle of the triumphal vehicle;

but, rebuking her for her hideous sins, my lady turned her into such fury as bones could endure without flesh.

Then, from where it had first come, I saw the eagle descend into the ark of the chariot and leave it feathered.

And as comes from a heart that is grieving, a voice came from the sky and said: "Oh, my little ship, how badly you are burdened!"

Then it seemed to me that the earth opened between both wheels, and I saw a dragon emerge, fixing his tail on the chariot.

And like a wasp that draws back its needle, drawing his malignant tail toward itself, he pulled himself up from the bottom and went on wandering.

What remained, like living earth from weeds, was covered by the feather, offered perhaps with a healthy and benign intention,

and both wheels and the rudder were covered with it, so that a sigh no longer holds the mouth open.

Thus transformed, the holy building thrust forth heads on all sides, three on the pole and one on each corner.

The first were horned like oxen, but the four had a single horn on their foreheads: no monster like it had ever been seen before.

Secure, like a rock on a high mountain, a loosed prostitute appeared to me, her brows ready;

and as if to prevent her from being taken from her, I saw a giant standing beside her; and they kissed each other sometimes.

But because she turned her greedy and wandering eye toward me, that ferocious lover lashed her from head to soles;

then, filled with suspicion and cruel rage, he unleashed the monster and dragged it through the forest, so that she alone served as a shield for me

against the prostitute and the new beast.

Purgatorio · Canto XXXIII

"God, let the people come," the ladies began, alternating now three and now four sweet psalms, and weeping;

and Beatrice, sighing and pious, listened so earnestly that Mary turned a little closer to the cross.

But after the other virgins gave her time to speak, she rose upright and replied, colored like fire:

"Modicum, et non videbitis me; et iterum, my beloved sisters, modicum, et vos videbitis me."

Then all seven of them placed themselves before her, and after her, only beckoning, moved me and the lady and the sage, who remained standing.

So she went; and I do not believe that her tenth step had been taken on the ground when she struck my eyes with hers;

And with a calm look, "Come more quickly," she said to me, "so that, if I speak to you, you may be well disposed to listen to me."

As soon as I was with her, as I should have been, she said to me: "Brother, why do you not dare to ask me now, coming with me?"

As with those who are too reverent before their elders in speaking, who do not raise their voices to their teeth,

it happened to me, who without a clear voice began: "Madonna, you know my need, and what is good for it."

And she to me: "From fear and shame I want you to free yourself from it, so that you no longer speak like a man who dreams.

Know that the vessel that the serpent broke was and is not; but whoever is to blame for it, believe that he fears not the vengeance of God.

The eagle that left its feathers on the chariot, becoming a monster and then prey, will not be without an heir for all time;

for I certainly see, and therefore I tell it, stars already approaching, safe from every obstacle and barrier,

in which a Five Hundred and Ten and Five, sent by God, will slay the thieve along with the giant who commits crimes with her.

And perhaps my dark narrative, like Themis and the Sphinx, will persuade you less, because it actuates the intellect in their own way;

but the Naiads will soon be the ones who will solve this difficult riddle without harming sheep or grain.

You note; and just as they are borne by me, so mark these words for the living, that life is a race toward death.

And remember, when you write them, not to conceal what you have seen of the tree that has now been stolen twice here.

Whoever steals this tree or tears it down, in fact offends God, who created it holy only for His use.

To bite it, in pain and desire for five thousand years and more, the first soul yearned for Him who punished the bite in itself.

Your genius is asleep, if it does not deem it, for a singular reason, to be so sublime and so swept away at its summit.

And if the vain thoughts around your mind had not been like Elsa water, and their pleasure like Pyramus to the mulberry,

for so many circumstances alone, you would morally recognize the justice of God, in the interdict, to the tree.

But because I see you in your intellect made of stone and, petrified, stained, so that the light of my words dazzles you,

I also want, and if not written, at least painted, that you carry it within you, as the staff of a palm girded carries it.

And I: "Like sealing wax, which does not change the imprinted image, my brain is now sealed by you.

But why does your desired word fly so far above my sight, that the more it helps itself, the more it loses it?"

"So that you may know," he said, "that school you have followed, and see its doctrine as it can follow my word;

and see your path as distant from the divine as the heaven that reaches highest is distant from earth."

Whereupon I replied to her: "I do not recall ever having estranged myself from you, nor do I have any conscience that remorses me."

"And if you cannot remember," she replied smiling, "now remember how you drank of Lethe even then;

and if from smoke one can deduce fire, this oblivion clearly concludes guilt in your desire, intent elsewhere.

Truly now my words will be naked, as much as it would be fitting to uncover them to your harsh sight."

And more brilliantly and with slower steps the sun held the circle of noon, which here and there, as one waits for it,

when they stopped, as one stops when one goes before a people escorting if he finds something new or its traces,

the seven women at the edge of a pale shadow, as the Alps carry beneath green leaves and black branches over their cold streams.

Before them I seemed to see Euphrates and Tigris issuing from a fountain and, as if friends, lazily parting.

"O light, O glory of the human race, what water is this that here unfolds from a source and is far from itself?"

At this prayer it was said to me: "Pray Matelda to tell you." And here the beautiful lady replied, as one does when freed from guilt:

"This and other things have been told to you for my sake; and I am sure that the waters of Lethe did not hide it from him."

And Beatrice: "Perhaps a greater care, which often deprives memory, has darkened his mind in his eyes.

But see Eunoe who is flowing there: lead him to it, and as you are accustomed, revive his stunned power."

As a gentle soul, who makes no excuses, but makes its will the will of another as soon as it is revealed by a sign;

so, after I was seized by her, the beautiful lady moved, and said to Statius in a womanly manner: "Come with him."

If I had, reader, more space to write, I would still sing in part of the sweet drink that would never have satiated me;

but since all the pages woven for this second canticle are full, the rein of art no longer allows me to go.

I returned from the most holy wave, restored like new plants reborn with new foliage,

pure and ready to ascend to the stars.

PARADISO
Paradiso · Canto I

The glory of Him who moves everything penetrates the universe, and shines more in one part and less in another.

I was in the heaven that receives most of His light, and I saw things that he who descends from there neither knows nor can recount;

because, approaching its desire, our intellect sinks so deeply that memory cannot follow it.

Truly, whatever I could treasure of the holy kingdom in my mind will now be the subject of my song.

O good Apollo, for my final work, make me a vessel of your power as you ask for the gift of your beloved laurel.

Up to now, one ridge of Parnassus has been enough for me; But now with both of you I must enter the remaining arena.

Enter my breast, and breathe upon me, as when you drew Marsyas from the womb of his limbs.

O divine power, if you lend me enough to reveal the shadow of the blessed kingdom imprinted on my head,

you will see me come to the foot of your beloved tree, and crown myself with the leaves that the material and you will make me worthy.

So rarely, father, are we gathered to triumph over Caesar or poet, the guilt and shame of human desires,

that the Penean leaf should give birth to joy in the joyful Delphic deity, when one thirsts for itself.

A small spark gives rise to a great flame: perhaps, behind me, prayers will be made with better voices for Cirrha to respond.

The world's lamp rises for mortals through various mouths; but from that which joins four circles with three crosses,

with a better course and a better star it emerges joined, and the worldly wax more in its own way tempers and seals.

It had made such a mouth on that side morning and on this side evening, and that hemisphere was almost entirely white, and the other part black,

when I saw Beatrice turned on her left flank and gazing at the sun: never before had an eagle fixed itself so fixedly upon her.

And just as a second ray is wont to issue from the first and rise upward, like a pilgrim who wishes to return,

so from his action, infused through my eyes into my imagination, my own became, and I fixed my eyes on the sun beyond our wont.

Much is permitted there that is not permitted here to our virtues, thanks to the place made for the human species.

I did not endure it long, nor so little, that I did not see it sparkle around, like boiling iron issuing from the fire;

and suddenly it seemed day to day had been added, as if he who can had adorned the heaven with another sun.

Beatrice stood with her eyes fixed entirely on the eternal wheels; and I fixed my eyes on her, from up there.

In his aspect I became within me as Glaucus did when tasting the grass that made him a companion in the sea of ​​the other gods.

Transhumanism cannot be expressed in words; therefore, let the example suffice, for whom experience holds grace.

If I was alone in myself that which you newly created, O Love who governs the heavens, you know it, for with your light you raised me.

When the wheel that you eternalize, desired forever, made me attentive to itself with the harmony you temper and discern,

then the sky seemed so lit by the flame of the sun that no rain or river made a lake so vast.

The novelty of the sound and the great light of their cause kindled in me a desire never before felt with such keenness.

Whereupon she, who saw me as I did, opened her mouth to calm my troubled soul, before I began to ask,

and began: "You make yourself so heavy with false imagination that you do not see what you would see if you had shaken it off.

You are not on earth, as you believe; but lightning, fleeing its proper place, did not run as you, who return to it."

If I was divested of my first doubt by the brief, smiling words, I was more entangled in a new one,

and I said: "You were already content with the repose of great admiration; but now I marvel how I transcend these light bodies."

Whereupon she, after a pious sigh, turned her eyes toward me with that look a mother makes over a delirious child,

and began: "All things have order among themselves, and this is the form that makes the universe resemble God.

Here the lofty creatures see the imprint of the eternal value, which is the end to which the aforementioned norm is made.

In the order I speak of, all natures are inclined, by different fates, closer and closer to their origin;

wherefore they move to different ports across the great sea of ​​being, and each with an instinct given to it that carries it.

This one carries the fire toward the moon; this one is a mover in mortal hearts; this one constrains and gathers the earth within itself;

nor does this bow shoot only those creatures that are without intelligence, but those that have intellect and love.

Providence, which so arranges, with its light keeps the heaven always calm, in which that which has the greatest haste turns;

and now there, as if decreed to a place, the power of that string which directs what it releases to a joyful signal carries us.

True it is that, just as form often does not accord with the intention of art, because the matter is deaf to respond,

so the creature sometimes deviates from this course, which has the power to bend, thus impelled, in another direction;

and just as one can see fire falling from a cloud, so the first impetus is swept to the ground by false pleasure.

You ought not, if I rightly judge, to admire your ascent, except as a stream descends from a high mountain to its bottom.

It would be a marvel to you if, free from hindrance, you had sat down, as if on earth at peace in living fire."

Then he turned his face toward the sky.

Paradiso · Canto II

O you who are in a small boat, eager to listen, follow my vessel as it passes singing,

return to revisit your shores: do not set sail, for perhaps, losing me, you would remain lost.

The water I take has never been traversed; Minerva breathes, and Apollo leads me, and nine Muses point out to me the Bears.

You few who have long stretched your necks to the bread of angels, with which we live here but never get satiated,

can well set your ship through the deep salt, keeping my furrow before the water that returns to evenness.

Those glorious ones who passed through Colchis were not as amazed as you will be when they saw Jason turned plowman.

The concrete and perpetual thirst for the divine realm bore us almost as swiftly as you see the heavens.

Beatrice was above, and I was gazing at her; and perhaps just as a diamond rests and flies and is being uncorked from its nut,

I saw myself arrive where a wondrous thing turned my gaze away; and therefore she, from whom my care could not be hidden,

turned toward me, as joyful as she was beautiful, "Direct your grateful mind to God," she said to me, "who has joined us to the first star."

It seemed to me that a cloud covered us, bright, thick, solid, and polished, as if struck by the sun.

Within itself the eternal pearl received us, as water receives a ray of light while remaining united.

If I were a body, and here it is not conceivable how one dimension can endure another, which must be if body returns to body,

the desire to see that essence in which we see how our nature and God are united should be more inflamed.

There will be seen what we hold by faith, not demonstrated, but will be known in itself as the first truth that man believes.

I replied: "My lady, as devoutly as I can be, I thank Him who has removed me from the mortal world.

But tell me: what are the dark marks on this body, which down there on earth make others tell tales of Cain?"

She smiled somewhat, and then said to me, "If the opinion of mortals errs where the key to sense does not unlock,

certainly the arrows of admiration should no longer pierce you, since you see that reason has short wings behind the senses.

But tell me what you think of it yourself." And I: "What appears different to us up here, I believe is caused by rare and dense bodies."

And she: "You will certainly see your belief submerged in falsehood, if you listen carefully to the argument I will make against it.

The eighth sphere shows you many lights, which can be noted in their different aspects both in their nature and in their magnitude.

If rare and dense were to do so, a single virtue would be in all, more or less distributed and equally distributed.

Different virtues must be the fruits of formal principles, and those, without one, would be destroyed by your reasoning.

Again, if that dark cause you ask were rare, either this planet would be partly devoid of its matter,

or, just as a body divides fat and lean, so this one would change its volume.

If the first were the case, it would be evident in the eclipse of the sun, because the light shines through as in another rare substance.

This is not the case: therefore, the other remains to be seen; and if it happens that I fail to see the other, your opinion will be falsified.

If it is true that this rare thing does not pass, there must be a limit beyond which its opposite no longer allows it to pass;

and then the other's ray is melted back, just as color returns through glass that hides lead behind it.

Now you will say that the ray appears darker there than in other places, because it is refracted further back there.

From this question, experience can determine, if you ever try it, which is usually the source of the streams of your arts.

You will take three mirrors; and remove the two from you in one way, and the other, further removed, find your eyes between the two first ones.

Turning to them, make sure that behind your back there is a light that lights the three mirrors and returns to you, reflected by all of them.

Although in the greater quantity your vision does not extend so far, there you will see how it must shine equally.

Now, just as the subject remains stripped of its former color and cold by the warm rays of the snow,

so, left in your intellect, I wish to inform you with a light so vivid that it will tremble in your sight.

Within the heaven of divine peace revolves a body in whose power the being of all its content lies.

The next heaven, which has so many visions, divides that being into diverse essences, distracted from it and contained by it.

The other circles, through various differences, the distinctions they have within themselves dispose to their ends and their seeds.

These organs of the world thus proceed, as you now see, from step to step, taking from above and acting from below.

Remember now how I go through this place to the truth you desire, so that you may then know how to hold the ford.

The motion and power of the holy circles, like the blacksmith's art of the hammer, must be inspired by the blessed motors;

and the heaven, which so many lights make beautiful, takes the image of the deep mind that turns it and makes itself a seal.

And as the soul resolves itself within your dust through different members and conformed to different powers,

so does its intelligence, multiplied by the stars, unfold its goodness, revolving above its unity.

Different virtues form different alloys with the precious body they animate, in which, as life in you, they bind themselves.

From the joyful nature from which they derive, the mixed virtue shines through the body like joy through a living pupil.

From it comes that which appears different from light to light, not from dense and rare; it is the formal principle that produces,

in conformity with its goodness, the turbulence and the brightness.

Paradiso · Canto III

That sun, which first warmed my breast with love, had revealed to me, testing and reproving, the sweet aspect of beautiful truth;

and I, to confess myself correctly and certainly, raised my head as far as was necessary to speak more erect;

but a vision appeared that held me so tightly to itself, to see itself, that it did not recall my confession.

As through transparent and polished glass, or through clear and tranquil waters, not so deep that the bottom is lost,

the notes of our faces return so faintly that a pearl on a white brow comes no less strongly to our pupils;

such I saw many faces ready to speak; whereupon I ran, in the opposite error, to that which kindled love between man and the fountain.

As soon as I noticed them, imagining their mirrored features, I turned my eyes to see whose they were.

And seeing nothing, I turned them straight forward into the light of the sweet Guide, who, smiling, burned in His holy eyes.

"Do not marvel that I smile," He said to me, "at your childish skirt, for I do not yet trust my foot to the truth,

but turn you, as is usual, to the void: true substances are what you see, bound here for lack of a vow.

Therefore, speak with them, and listen, and believe; for the true light that satisfies them of itself does not allow them to turn their feet."

And I turned to the shade that seemed most eager to speak, and began, almost like one deceived by excessive desire:

"O well-created spirit, who in the rays of eternal life sense the sweetness that, untasted, can never be understood,

it will be gracious to me if you content me with your name and your fate." Then she, prompt and with laughing eyes:

"Our charity closes no doors to just will, except as one that wants all its court to be similar to itself.

I was a virgin sister in the world; and if your mind considers itself well, it will not hide from you that I am more beautiful,

but you will recognize that I am Piccarda, who, placed here with these other blessed ones, am blessed in the slowest sphere.

Our affections, which are inflamed only by the pleasure of the Holy Spirit, rejoice in his order formed.

And this fate, which seems so low, is given to us because our desires were neglected and empty in some corner.

And I to her: "In your marvelous aspects shines something divine that transforms you from your first concepts:

Therefore I was not ready to remember; but now what you tell me helps me, so that it is more difficult for me to imagine.

But tell me: you who are happy here, do you desire a higher place to see more and to make more friends?"

She first smiled a little with those other shades; then she answered me so joyfully that she seemed to burn with love in the first fire:

"Brother, our will is quieted by the virtue of charity, which makes us want only what we have, and thirsts for nothing else.

If we desired to be more sublime, our desires would be discordant with the will of Him who chooses us here;

which you will see does not fit in these circles, if being in charity is necessary here, and if you consider its nature well.

Indeed, it is fitting for this blessed one to remain within the divine will, so that our very desires become one;

so that, as we pass from threshold to threshold through this kingdom, the entire kingdom pleases as it pleases the king who wills us in his will.

And in his will is our peace: it is that sea to which everything she creates or nature makes moves."

It was clear to me then that everywhere in heaven there is paradise, and the grace of the supreme good does not rain there in one way.

But as it happens, if one food satiates and the appetite for another still remains, that one is desired and for that one is thanked,

so I did with action and word, to learn from her what was the web from which she did not draw the shuttle.

"Perfect life and high merit in heaven, a lady higher up," she said to me, "by whose rule in your world she dresses and veils herself,

so that until death she may see and sleep with that husband who accepts every vow that conforms charity to his pleasure.

From the world, to follow her, I fled as a young girl, and in her dress I enclosed myself and promised the path of her sect.

Then men, accustomed to evil rather than good, snatched me from the sweet cloister: God knows what my life was then cast.

And this other splendor that appears to you on my right side and that is kindled with all the light of our sphere,

what I say of myself, understands of itself: she was a sister, and thus the shadow of the sacred shrouds was taken from her head.

But since she was turned back to the world against her will and against good custom, she was never freed from the veil of her heart.

This is the light of great Constance, who from the second wind of Sweetness generated the third and final power.

Thus she spoke to me, and then began singing, "Ave, Maria," and singing, I vanished like a heavy thing through dark water.

My sight, which I followed her as long as possible, after losing her, turned to the mark of greater desire,

and turned entirely to Beatrice; but she struck my gaze so that at first my face could not bear it;

and this made me slower to ask.

Paradiso · Canto IV

Between two foods, distant and moving in the same way, one would die of hunger before a free man could bring one to his teeth;

Thus would a lamb stand between the lusts of two fierce wolves, equally fearful; thus would a dog stand between two ladies.

Therefore, if I remained silent, I do not reproach myself, driven by my doubts in a way, since it was necessary, nor do I praise it.

I remained silent, but my desire was painted in my face, and the questioning with it was far more ardent than distinct speech.

Beatrice acted as Daniel did, relieving Nebuchadnezzar of the anger that had unjustly made him wicked;

and said: "I see clearly how one desire and another draw you, so that your care binds itself so that it cannot escape.

You argue: 'If good will endures, why does the violence of others diminish my measure of merit?'"

Still, the fact that souls seem to return to the stars, according to Plato's saying, gives you cause for doubt.

These are the questions that equally weigh on your will; therefore, I will first discuss the one that is most poignant.

Of the Seraphim, he who is most inflamed, Moses, Samuel, and that John whom you wish to take, I mean, not Mary,

have their seats in no other heaven than these spirits who just appeared to you, nor do they have more or less years to live;

but they all make their first circle beautiful, and have different sweet lives, feeling more or less the eternal breath.

They have shown themselves here, not because this sphere has been sprung for them, but to give a sign of the celestial one that has ascended less.

Speaking thus is fitting for your intellect, since only from the sensible can one apprehend what is later worthy of the intellect.

Therefore Scripture condescends to your discretion, and attributes feet and hands to God and means something else;

and Holy Church, with human aspect, represents Gabriel and Michael, and the other whom Tobias restored to health.

What Timaeus argues about souls is not similar to what is seen here, because, as he says, he seems to feel.

He says that the soul returns to its star, believing that it was therefore determined when nature gave it its form;

and perhaps his meaning is different from the sound of the voice, and may be intended not to be mocked.

If he intends to return the honor of influence and the blame to these wheels, perhaps he strikes some truth in his bow.

This principle, misunderstood, has already distorted almost the entire world, so much so that it has passed from naming Jupiter, Mercury, and Mars.

The other doubt that moves you is less venomous, because its malice could not lead you away from me.

For our justice to appear unjust in the eyes of mortals is an argument of faith and not of heretical wickedness.

But since your understanding can penetrate this truth, as you desire, I will make you content.

If violence is when the one who suffers yields nothing to what he forces, these souls were not excused by it:
